	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">New Age</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the New Age movement. For the astrological age in western astrology, see <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Aquarius" title="Age of Aquarius">Age of Aquarius</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/New_Age_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="New Age (disambiguation)">New Age (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG/220px-Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG/330px-Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG/440px-Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2016" data-file-height="1512" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A New Age "Rainbow Gathering" in Bosnia in 2007</div></div></div>
<p><b>New Age</b> is a term applied to a range of <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> or <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious</a> beliefs and practices that developed in <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western nations</a> during the 1970s. Precise scholarly definitions of the New Age differ in their emphasis, largely as a result of its highly <a href="/wiki/Eclecticism" title="Eclecticism">eclectic</a> structure. Although analytically often considered to be religious, those involved in it typically prefer the designation of <b>spiritual</b> or <b>Mind, Body, Spirit</b> and rarely use the term <i>New Age</i> themselves. Many scholars of the subject refer to it as the <b>New Age movement</b>, although others contest this term and suggest that it is better seen as a <a href="/wiki/Milieu" class="mw-redirect" title="Milieu">milieu</a> or <a href="/wiki/Zeitgeist" title="Zeitgeist">zeitgeist</a>.
</p><p>As a form of <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism" title="Western esotericism">Western esotericism</a>, the New Age drew heavily upon a number of older esoteric traditions, in particular those that emerged from the <a href="/wiki/Occultism" class="mw-redirect" title="Occultism">occultist</a> current that developed in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries. Such prominent occultist influences include the work of <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Swedenborg" title="Emanuel Swedenborg">Emanuel Swedenborg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Mesmer" title="Franz Mesmer">Franz Mesmer</a>, as well as the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">Spiritualism</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Thought" title="New Thought">New Thought</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theosophy_(Blavatskian)" title="Theosophy (Blavatskian)">Theosophy</a>. A number of mid-twentieth century influences, such as the <a href="/wiki/UFO_religions" class="mw-redirect" title="UFO religions">UFO religions</a> of the 1950s, the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture of the 1960s</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Human_Potential_Movement" title="Human Potential Movement">Human Potential Movement</a>, also exerted a strong influence on the early development of the New Age. The exact origins of the phenomenon remain contested, but there is general agreement that it developed in the 1970s, at which time it was centred largely in the United Kingdom. It expanded and grew largely in the 1980s and 1990s, in particular within the United States. By the start of the 21st century, the term <i>New Age</i> was increasingly rejected within this milieu, with some scholars arguing that the New Age phenomenon had ended.
</p><p>Despite its highly eclectic nature, a number of beliefs commonly found within the New Age have been identified. <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">Theologically</a>, the New Age typically adopts a belief in a <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holistic</a> form of divinity that imbues all of the universe, including human beings themselves. There is thus a strong emphasis on the spiritual authority of the self. This is accompanied by a common belief in a wide variety of semi-divine non-human entities, such as <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Masters_of_the_Ancient_Wisdom" title="Masters of the Ancient Wisdom">masters</a>, with whom humans can communicate, particularly through the form of <a href="/wiki/Mediumship#Channeling" title="Mediumship">channeling</a>. Typically viewing human history as being divided into a series of distinct ages, a common New Age belief is that whereas once humanity lived in an age of great technological advancement and spiritual wisdom, it has entered a period of spiritual degeneracy, which will be remedied through the establishment of a coming <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Aquarius" title="Age of Aquarius">Age of Aquarius</a>, from which the milieu gets its name. There is also a strong focus on healing, particularly using forms of <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">alternative medicine</a>, and an emphasis on the notion that spirituality and <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> can be unified.
</p><p>Centered primarily in Western countries, those involved in the New Age have predominantly been from middle and upper-middle-class backgrounds. The degree to which New Agers are involved in the milieu varied considerably, from those who adopted a number of New Age ideas and practices to those who fully embraced and dedicated their lives to it. The New Age has generated criticism from established Christian organisations as well as <a href="/wiki/Modern_Paganism" title="Modern Paganism">modern Pagan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous communities</a>. From the 1990s onward, the New Age became the subject of research by academic scholars of <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">religious studies</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"One of the few things on which all scholars agree concerning New Age is that it is difficult to define. Often, the definition given actually reflects the background of the scholar giving the definition. Thus, the New Ager views New Age as a revolutionary period of history dictated by the stars; the Christian apologist has often defined new age as a cult; the historian of ideas understands it as a manifestation of the perennial tradition; the philosopher sees New Age as a monistic or holistic worldview; the sociologist describes New Age as a new religious movement (NRM); while the psychologist describes it as a form of narcissism."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Scholar of religion Daren Kemp, 2004<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp20041_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp20041-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The New Age phenomenon has proved difficult to define,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001363Kemp20041Granholm201359_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001363Kemp20041Granholm201359-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> with much scholarly disagreement as to its scope.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGranholm201359_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranholm201359-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The scholars Steven J. Sutcliffe and Ingvild Sælid Gilhus have even suggested that it remains "among the most disputed of categories in the study of religion".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffeGilhus20131_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffeGilhus20131-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The scholar of religion <a href="/wiki/Paul_Heelas" title="Paul Heelas">Paul Heelas</a> characterised the New Age as "an eclectic hotch-potch of beliefs, practices, and ways of life" that can be identified as a singular phenomenon through their use of "the same (or very similar) <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> to do with the human (and planetary) condition and how it can be <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_transformation" title="Spiritual transformation">transformed</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas19961–2_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas19961–2-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the historian of religion <a href="/wiki/Olav_Hammer" title="Olav Hammer">Olav Hammer</a> termed it "a common denominator for a variety of quite divergent contemporary popular practices and beliefs" that have emerged since the late 1970s and are "largely united by historical links, a shared discourse and an <i>air de famille</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer2006855_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer2006855-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> According to Hammer, this New Age was a "fluid and fuzzy <a href="/wiki/Cult" title="Cult">cultic</a> milieu".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200114_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200114-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_religion" title="Sociology of religion">sociologist of religion</a> <a href="/wiki/Michael_York_(religious_studies_scholar)" title="Michael York (religious studies scholar)">Michael York</a> described the New Age as "an umbrella term that includes a great variety of groups and identities" that are united by their "expectation of a major and universal change being primarily founded on the individual and collective development of human potential."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork19951–2_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork19951–2-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The scholar of religion <a href="/wiki/Wouter_Hanegraaff" title="Wouter Hanegraaff">Wouter Hanegraaff</a> adopted a different approach by asserting that "New Age" was "a <i>label</i> attached indiscriminately to whatever seems to fit it" and that as a result it "means very different things to different people".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff19961_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff19961-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> He thus argued against the idea that the New Age could be considered "a unified ideology or <i><a href="/wiki/World_view" title="World view">Weltanschauung</a></i>",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996515_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996515-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> although he believed that it could be considered a "more or less unified 'movement'."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996522_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996522-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Other scholars have suggested that the New Age is too diverse to be a singular <i>movement</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199824Sutcliffe2003a9Chryssides200722_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199824Sutcliffe2003a9Chryssides200722-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The scholar of religion <a href="/wiki/George_D._Chryssides" class="mw-redirect" title="George D. Chryssides">George D. Chryssides</a> called it "a counter-cultural <i>Zeitgeist</i>",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides200722_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides200722-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> while the sociologist of religion Steven Bruce suggested that New Age was a <a href="/wiki/Milieu" class="mw-redirect" title="Milieu">milieu</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199824_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199824-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Heelas and scholar of religion <a href="/wiki/Linda_Woodhead" title="Linda Woodhead">Linda Woodhead</a> called it the "holistic milieu".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead20058_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead20058-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no central authority within the New Age phenomenon that can determine what counts as New Age and what does not.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001364_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001364-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
Many of those groups and individuals who could analytically be categorised as part of the New Age reject the term <i>New Age</i> in reference to themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis19921–2Heelas199617York2001364Sutcliffe2003a200Partridge20042MacKian20125_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis19921–2Heelas199617York2001364Sutcliffe2003a200Partridge20042MacKian20125-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Some even express active hostility to the term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp2004177_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp2004177-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Rather than terming themselves <i>New Agers</i>, those involved in this milieu commonly describe themselves as spiritual "seekers",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a200_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a200-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and some self-identify as a member of a different religious group, such as Christianity, Judaism, or Buddhism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike200425_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike200425-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In 2003 Sutcliffe observed that the use of the term <i>New Age</i> was "optional, episodic and declining overall", adding that among the very few individuals who did use it, they usually did so with qualification, for instance by placing it in quotation marks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a197_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a197-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Other academics, such as Sara MacKian, have argued that the sheer diversity of the New Age renders the term too problematic for scholars to use.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides200710MacKian20127_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides200710MacKian20127-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> MacKian proposed "everyday spirituality" as an alternate term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian201212_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian201212-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While acknowledging that <i>New Age</i> was a problematic term, the scholar of religion  <a href="/wiki/James_R._Lewis_(scholar)" title="James R. Lewis (scholar)">James R. Lewis</a> stated that it remained a useful <a href="/wiki/Etic" class="mw-redirect" title="Etic">etic</a> category for scholars to use because, "There exists no comparable term which covers all aspects of the movement."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis19922_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis19922-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, Chryssides argued that the fact that "New Age" is a "theoretical concept" does not "undermine its usefulness or employability"; he drew comparisons with "<a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>", a similar "western etic piece of vocabulary" that scholars of religion used despite its problems.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides200713_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides200713-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Religion.2C_spirituality.2C_and_esotericism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion,_spirituality,_and_esotericism">Religion, spirituality, and esotericism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Religion, spirituality, and esotericism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In discussing the New Age, academics have varyingly referred to "New Age spirituality" and "New Age religion".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp20041_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp20041-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Those involved in the New Age rarely consider it to be "religion"—negatively associating that term solely with <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religion</a>—and instead describe their <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_practice" title="Spiritual practice">practices</a> as "spirituality".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a214–215Partridge200448_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a214–215Partridge200448-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Religious studies scholars, however, have repeatedly referred to the New Age milieu as a "religion".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996243Partridge200438_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996243Partridge200438-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> York described the New Age as a <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">new religious movement</a> (NRM).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork19952_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork19952-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Conversely, both Heelas and Sutcliffe rejected this categorisation;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas19969Sutcliffe2003a200_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas19969Sutcliffe2003a200-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Heelas believed that while elements of the New Age represented NRMs, this did not apply to every New Age group.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas19969_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas19969-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, Chryssides stated that the New Age could not be seen as "a religion" in itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides200719_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides200719-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"The New Age movement is the cultic milieu having become conscious of itself, in the later 1970s, as constituting a more or less unified "movement". All manifestations of this movement are characterized by a popular western culture criticism expressed in terms of a secularized esotericism."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Scholar of esotericism Wouter Hanegraaff, 1996<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996522_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996522-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The New Age is also a form of <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism" title="Western esotericism">Western esotericism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199533Hanegraaff1996400Hammer20019_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199533Hanegraaff1996400Hammer20019-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Hanegraaff regarded the New Age as a form of "popular culture criticism", in that it represented a reaction against the dominant Western values of <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a> religion and rationalism,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996331_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996331-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> adding that "New Age religion formulates such criticism not at random, but falls back on" the ideas of earlier Western esoteric groups.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996515_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996515-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The New Age has also been identified by various scholars of religion as part of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cultic_milieu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cultic milieu (page does not exist)">cultic milieu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996522Hammer200128Chryssides200717_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996522Hammer200128Chryssides200717-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This concept, developed by the sociologist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colin_Campbell_(sociologist)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Colin Campbell (sociologist) (page does not exist)">Colin Campbell</a>, refers to a social network of marginalised ideas. Through their shared marginalisation within a given society, these disparate ideas interact and create new syntheses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200128–29_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200128–29-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hammer identified much of the New Age as corresponding to the concept of "<a href="/wiki/Folk_religions" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk religions">folk religions</a>" in that it seeks to deal with <a href="/wiki/Existential" class="mw-redirect" title="Existential">existential</a> questions regarding subjects like death and disease in "an unsystematic fashion, often through a process of <a href="/wiki/Bricolage" title="Bricolage">bricolage</a> from already available narratives and rituals".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer2006855_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer2006855-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> York also heuristically divides the New Age into three broad trends. The first, the <i>social camp</i>, represents groups that primarily seek to bring about social change, while the second, the <i>occult camp</i>, instead focus on contact with spirit entities and channeling. York's third group, the <i>spiritual camp</i>, represents a middle ground between these two camps that focuses largely on <a href="/wiki/Self-development" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-development">individual development</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199536–37_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199536–37-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term <i>new age</i>, along with related terms like <i>new era</i> and <i>new world</i>, long predate the emergence of the New Age movement, and have widely been used to assert that a better way of life for humanity is dawning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199615_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199615-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> It occurs commonly, for instance, in political contexts; the <a href="/wiki/Great_Seal_of_the_United_States" title="Great Seal of the United States">Great Seal of the United States</a>, designed in 1782, proclaims a "new order of ages", while in the 1980s the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> Premier <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a> proclaimed that "all mankind is entering a new age".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199615_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199615-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The term has also appeared within Western esoteric schools of thought, having a scattered use from the mid-nineteenth century onward.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a25_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a25-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  In 1864 the American <a href="/wiki/Swedenborgian" class="mw-redirect" title="Swedenborgian">Swedenborgian</a> <a href="/wiki/Warren_Felt_Evans" title="Warren Felt Evans">Warren Felt Evans</a> published <i>The New Age and its Message</i>, while in 1907 <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Orage" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfred Orage">Alfred Orage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Holbrook_Jackson" title="Holbrook Jackson">Holbrook Jackson</a> began editing a weekly journal of <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">Christian liberalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Age" title="The New Age">The New Age</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199617_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199617-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The concept of a coming "new age" that would be inaugurated by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">return to Earth</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> was a theme in the poetry of <a href="/wiki/Wellesley_Tudor_Pole" title="Wellesley Tudor Pole">Wellesley Tudor Pole</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johanna_Brandt" title="Johanna Brandt">Johanna Brandt</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a26_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a26-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and then also appeared in the work of the American Theosophist <a href="/wiki/Alice_Bailey" title="Alice Bailey">Alice Bailey</a>, who used the term prominently in such titles as <i>Disciplineship in the New Age</i> (1944) and <i>Education in the New Age</i> (1954).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a26_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a26-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between the 1930s and 1960s a small number of groups and individuals became preoccupied with the concept of a coming "New Age" and prominently used the term accordingly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a55_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a55-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The term had thus become a recurring motif in the esoteric spirituality milieu.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a99_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a99-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
Sutcliffe therefore expressed the view that while the term <i>New Age</i> had originally been an "apocalyptic emblem", it would only be later that it became "a tag or codeword for a 'spiritual' idiom".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a29_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a29-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antecedents">Antecedents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Antecedents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption-center{text-align:center;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:289px;max-width:289px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:143px;max-width:143px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Helena_Petrovna_Blavatsky.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Helena_Petrovna_Blavatsky.jpg/141px-Helena_Petrovna_Blavatsky.jpg" decoding="async" width="141" height="209" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Helena_Petrovna_Blavatsky.jpg/212px-Helena_Petrovna_Blavatsky.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Helena_Petrovna_Blavatsky.jpg/282px-Helena_Petrovna_Blavatsky.jpg 2x" data-file-width="295" data-file-height="438" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:CGJung.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/CGJung.jpg/140px-CGJung.jpg" decoding="async" width="140" height="210" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/CGJung.jpg/210px-CGJung.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/CGJung.jpg/280px-CGJung.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2604" data-file-height="3909" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="background-color:transparent">Prominent esoteric thinkers who influenced the New Age include <a href="/wiki/Helena_Blavatsky" title="Helena Blavatsky">Helena Blavatsky</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a> (right).</div></div></div></div>
<p>According to scholar <a href="/wiki/Nevill_Drury" title="Nevill Drury">Nevill Drury</a>, the New Age has a "tangible history",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrury200410_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrury200410-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> although Hanegraaff expressed the view that most New Agers were "surprisingly ignorant about the actual historical roots of their beliefs".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996323_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996323-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, Hammer thought that "source amnesia" was a "building block of a New Age worldview", with New Agers typically adopting ideas with no awareness of where those ideas originated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer2001180_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer2001180-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a form of Western esotericism,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199533_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199533-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> the New Age has antecedents that stretch back to southern Europe in <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllwood199259_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllwood199259-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> in 18th century Europe, new esoteric ideas developed in response to the development of scientific rationality. Scholars call this new esoteric trend <i><a href="/wiki/Occultism" class="mw-redirect" title="Occultism">occultism</a></i>, and this occultism was a key factor in the development of the worldview from which the New Age emerged.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996406–407_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996406–407-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the earliest influences on the New Age was the Swedish 18th century <a href="/wiki/Christian_mysticism" title="Christian mysticism">Christian mystic</a> <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Swedenborg" title="Emanuel Swedenborg">Emanuel Swedenborg</a>, who professed the ability to communicate with angels, demons, and spirits. Swedenborg's attempt to unite science and religion and his prediction of a coming era in particular have been cited as ways that he prefigured the New Age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander199231Hanegraaff1996424–429Kemp200442_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander199231Hanegraaff1996424–429Kemp200442-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Another early influence was the late 18th and early 19th century German physician and <a href="/wiki/Hypnotist" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypnotist">hypnotist</a> <a href="/wiki/Franz_Mesmer" title="Franz Mesmer">Franz Mesmer</a>, who claimed the existence of a force known as "<a href="/wiki/Animal_magnetism" title="Animal magnetism">animal magnetism</a>" running through the human body.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander199231Hanegraaff1996430–435Kemp200441_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander199231Hanegraaff1996430–435Kemp200441-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The establishment of <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">Spiritualism</a>, an occult religion influenced by both Swedenborgianism and Mesmerism, in the U.S. during the 1840s has also been identified as a precursor to the New Age, in particular through its rejection of established Christianity, its claims to representing a scientific approach to religion, and its emphasis on channeling spirit entities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996435Pike200424Kemp200437_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996435Pike200424Kemp200437-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"Most of the beliefs which characterise the New Age were already present by the end of the 19th century, even to such an extent that one may legitimately wonder whether the New Age brings anything new at all."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Historian of religion <a href="/wiki/Wouter_Hanegraaff" title="Wouter Hanegraaff">Wouter Hanegraaff</a>, 1996<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996482–483_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996482–483-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>A further major influence on the New Age was the <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a>, an occult group co-founded by the Russian <a href="/wiki/Helena_Blavatsky" title="Helena Blavatsky">Helena Blavatsky</a> in the late 19th century. In her books <i><a href="/wiki/Isis_Unveiled" title="Isis Unveiled">Isis Unveiled</a></i> (1877) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Doctrine" title="The Secret Doctrine">The Secret Doctrine</a></i> (1888), Blavatsky claimed that her Society was conveying the essence of all world religions, and it thus emphasized a focus on <a href="/wiki/Comparative_religion" title="Comparative religion">comparative religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander199231Hanegraaff1996448–455Pike200424Kemp200439_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander199231Hanegraaff1996448–455Pike200424Kemp200439-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Serving as a partial bridge between Theosophical ideas and those of the New Age was the American esotericist <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Cayce" title="Edgar Cayce">Edgar Cayce</a>, who founded the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Research_and_Enlightenment" title="Association for Research and Enlightenment">Association for Research and Enlightenment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199560Hammer200166_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199560Hammer200166-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Another influence was <a href="/wiki/New_Thought" title="New Thought">New Thought</a>, which developed in late nineteenth century <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> as a Christian-oriented healing movement before spreading throughout the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander199235Hanegraaff1996455–462Kemp200438_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander199235Hanegraaff1996455–462Kemp200438-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Another prominent influence was the psychologist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199646–47Hammer200169–70_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199646–47Hammer200169–70-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> 
Drury also identified as an important influence upon the New Age the Indian <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a>, an adherent of the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> who first brought Hinduism to the West in the late 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrury200427–28_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrury200427–28-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hanegraaff believed that the New Age's direct antecedents could be found in the <a href="/wiki/UFO_religion" title="UFO religion">UFO religions</a> of the 1950s, which he termed a "proto-New Age movement".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199695–96_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199695–96-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Many of these new religious movements had strong apocalyptic beliefs regarding a coming new age, which they typically asserted would be brought about by contact with extraterrestrials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199695–96Sutcliffe2003a72_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199695–96Sutcliffe2003a72-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Examples of such groups included the <a href="/wiki/Aetherius_Society" title="Aetherius Society">Aetherius Society</a>, founded in the UK in 1955, and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heralds_of_the_New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Heralds of the New Age (page does not exist)">Heralds of the New Age</a>, established in New Zealand in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a72,_74_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a72,_74-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barrel_House.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Wooden barrel house in forest with surrounding footpaths" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Barrel_House.jpg/200px-Barrel_House.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Barrel_House.jpg/300px-Barrel_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Barrel_House.jpg/400px-Barrel_House.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barrel_House.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This barrel house was the first dwelling constructed at the <a href="/wiki/Findhorn_Ecovillage" title="Findhorn Ecovillage">Findhorn Ecovillage</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1960s">1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From a historical perspective, the New Age phenomenon is rooted in the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture of the 1960s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199611Pike200415_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199611Pike200415-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> According to author Andrew Grant Jackson, <a href="/wiki/George_Harrison" title="George Harrison">George Harrison</a>'s adoption of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> philosophy and Indian instrumentation in his songs with <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a> in the mid 1960s, together with the band's highly publicised study of <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_Meditation" title="Transcendental Meditation">Transcendental Meditation</a>, "truly kick-started" the Human Potential Movement that subsequently became New Age.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Although not common throughout the counterculture, usage of the terms <i>New Age</i> and <i>Age of Aquarius</i> – used in reference to a coming era – were found within it,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a108–109_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a108–109-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> for instance appearing on adverts for the <a href="/wiki/Woodstock" title="Woodstock">Woodstock</a> festival of 1969,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a109_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a109-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and in the lyrics of "<a href="/wiki/Aquarius_(song)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquarius (song)">Aquarius</a>", the opening song of the 1967 musical <i><a href="/wiki/Hair_(musical)" title="Hair (musical)">Hair: The American Tribal Love-Rock Musical</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199610Sutcliffe2003a109SutcliffeGilhus20134_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199610Sutcliffe2003a109SutcliffeGilhus20134-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
This decade also witnessed the emergence of a variety of new religious movements and newly established religions in the United States, creating a spiritual milieu from which the New Age drew upon; these included the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Zen_Center" title="San Francisco Zen Center">San Francisco Zen Center</a>, Transcendental Meditation, <a href="/wiki/Soka_Gakkai" title="Soka Gakkai">Soka Gakkai</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Inner_Peace_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Inner Peace Movement (page does not exist)">Inner Peace Movement</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_All_Worlds" title="Church of All Worlds">Church of All Worlds</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Satan" title="Church of Satan">Church of Satan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199653–54_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199653–54-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Although there had been an established interest in Asian religious ideas in the U.S. from at least the eighteenth-century,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown199288–89_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown199288–89-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> many of these new developments were variants of Hinduism, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>, which had been imported to the West from Asia following the U.S. government's decision to rescind the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Exclusion_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Asian Exclusion Act">Asian Exclusion Act</a> in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199220Heelas199654–55_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199220Heelas199654–55-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 1962 the <a href="/wiki/Esalen_Institute" title="Esalen Institute">Esalen Institute</a> was established in <a href="/wiki/Big_Sur" title="Big Sur">Big Sur</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander199236–37York199535Hanegraaff199638–39Heelas199651_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander199236–37York199535Hanegraaff199638–39Heelas199651-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Esalen and similar personal growth centers had developed links to <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_psychology" title="Humanistic psychology">humanistic psychology</a>, and from this, the <a href="/wiki/Human_potential_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Human potential movement">human potential movement</a> emerged, strongly influenced the New Age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander199236,_41–43York19958Heelas199653_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander199236,_41–43York19958Heelas199653-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Britain, a number of small religious groups that came to be identified as the "light" movement had begun declaring the existence of a coming new age, influenced strongly by the Theosophical ideas of Blavatsky and Bailey.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199220_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199220-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The most prominent of these groups was the <a href="/wiki/Findhorn_Foundation" title="Findhorn Foundation">Findhorn Foundation</a>, which founded the Findhorn Ecovillage in the Scottish area of <a href="/wiki/Findhorn" title="Findhorn">Findhorn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moray" title="Moray">Moray</a> in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199220York199535Hanegraaff199638–39Heelas199651Chryssides20078_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199220York199535Hanegraaff199638–39Heelas199651Chryssides20078-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Although its founders were from an older generation, Findhorn attracted increasing numbers of countercultural baby boomers during the 1960s, to the extent that its population had grown sixfold to c. 120 residents by 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a118_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a118-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In October 1965, the founder of Findhorn, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Caddy" title="Peter Caddy">Peter Caddy</a>, attended a meeting of various prominent figures within Britain's esoteric milieu; titled "The Significance of the Group in the New Age", it was held at <a href="/wiki/Attingham_Park" title="Attingham Park">Attingham Park</a> over the course of a weekend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a83–84_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a83–84-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All of these groups created the backdrop from which the New Age movement emerged. As James R. Lewis and <a href="/wiki/J._Gordon_Melton" title="J. Gordon Melton">J. Gordon Melton</a> point out, the New Age phenomenon represents "a synthesis of many different preexisting movements and strands of thought".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisMelton1992xi_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisMelton1992xi-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, York asserted that while the New Age bore many similarities with both earlier forms of Western esotericism and Asian religion, it remained "distinct from its predecessors in its own self-consciousness as a new way of thinking".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork19951_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork19951-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Emergence_and_development:_c._1970.E2.80.932000"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_and_development:_c._1970–2000">Emergence and development: c. 1970–2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Emergence and development: c. 1970–2000">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"The late 1950s saw the first stirrings within the cultic milieu of a belief in a coming new age. A variety of small movements arose, revolving around revealed messages from beings in space and presenting a synthesis of post-Theosophical and other esoteric doctrines. These movements might have remained marginal, had it not been for the explosion of the counterculture in the 1960s and early 1970s. Various historical threads ... began to converge: nineteenth century doctrinal elements such as Theosophy and post-Theosophical esotericism as well as harmonious or positive thinking were now eclectically combined with ... religious psychologies: transpersonal psychology, Jungianism and a variety of Eastern teachings. It became perfectly feasible for the same individuals to consult the I Ching, practice Jungian astrology, read Abraham Maslow's writings on peak experiences, etc. The reason for the ready incorporation of such disparate sources was a similar goal of exploring an individualized and largely non-Christian religiosity."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Scholar of esotericism Olav Hammer, 2001<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200173_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200173-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>By the early 1970s, use of the term <i>New Age</i> was increasingly common within the cultic milieu.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200173_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200173-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> This was because—according to Sutcliffe—the "emblem" of the "New Age" had been passed from the "subcultural pioneers" in groups like Findhorn to the wider array of "countercultural baby boomers" between c. 1967 and 1974. He noted that as this happened, the meaning of the term <i>New Age</i> changed; whereas it had once referred specifically to a coming era, at this point it came to be used in a wider sense to refer to a variety of spiritual activities and practices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a112_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a112-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In the latter part of the 1970s, the New Age expanded to cover a wide variety of alternative spiritual and religious beliefs and practices, not all of which explicitly held to the belief in the Age of Aquarius, but were nevertheless widely recognised as broadly similar in their search for "alternatives" to mainstream society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In doing so, the "New Age" became a banner under which to bring together the wider "cultic milieu" of American society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199533_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199533-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The counterculture of the 1960s had rapidly declined by the start of the 1970s, in large part due to the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Commune" title="Commune">commune</a> movement,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199654_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199654-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> but it would be many former members of the counter-culture and <a href="/wiki/Hippie" title="Hippie">hippie</a> subculture who subsequently became early adherents of the New Age movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisMelton1992xi_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisMelton1992xi-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
The exact origins of the New Age movement remain an issue of debate; Melton asserted that it emerged in the early 1970s,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199218_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199218-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> whereas Hanegraaff instead traced its emergence to the latter 1970s, adding that it then entered its full development in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199612_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199612-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> This early form of the movement was based largely in Britain and exhibited a strong influence from Theosophy and <a href="/wiki/Anthroposophy" title="Anthroposophy">Anthroposophy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Hanegraaff termed this early core of the movement the <i>New Age sensu stricto</i>, or "New Age in the strict sense".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697SutcliffeGilhus20134_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697SutcliffeGilhus20134-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hanegraaff terms the broader development the <i>New Age sensu lato</i>, or "New Age in the wider sense".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697SutcliffeGilhus20134_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697SutcliffeGilhus20134-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Stores that came to be known as "New Age shops" opened up, selling related books, magazines, jewellery, and crystals, and they were typified by the playing of New Age music and the smell of incense.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a126–127_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a126–127-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>This probably influenced several thousand small metaphysical book- and gift-stores that increasingly defined themselves as "New Age bookstores",<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>  while New Age titles came to be increasingly available from mainstream bookstores and then websites like <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon.com">Amazon.com</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike200416_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike200416-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Not everyone who came to be associated with the New Age phenomenon openly embraced the term <i>New Age</i>, although it was popularised in books like <a href="/wiki/David_Spangler" title="David Spangler">David Spangler</a>'s 1977 work <i>Revelation: The Birth of a New Age</i> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Satin" title="Mark Satin">Mark Satin</a>'s 1979 book <i>New Age Politics: Healing Self and Society</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a124_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a124-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Ferguson" title="Marilyn Ferguson">Marilyn Ferguson</a>'s 1982 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Aquarian_Conspiracy" class="mw-redirect" title="The Aquarian Conspiracy">The Aquarian Conspiracy</a></i> has also been regarded as a landmark work in the development of the New Age, promoting the idea that a new era was emerging.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides20079_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides20079-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Other terms that were employed synonymously with <i>New Age</i> in this milieu included "Green", "Holistic", "Alternative", and "Spiritual".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a128_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a128-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>1971 witnessed the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Erhard_Seminars_Training" title="Erhard Seminars Training">est</a> by <a href="/wiki/Werner_H._Erhard" class="mw-redirect" title="Werner H. Erhard">Werner H. Erhard</a>, a transformational training course that became a prominent part of the early movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199658–60_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199658–60-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Melton suggested that the 1970s witnessed the growth of a relationship between the New Age movement and the older New Thought movement, as evidenced by the widespread use of <a href="/wiki/Helen_Schucman" title="Helen Schucman">Helen Schucman</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Course_in_Miracles" title="A Course in Miracles">A Course in Miracles</a></i> (1975), New Age music, and crystal healing in New Thought churches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199225–26_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199225–26-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Some figures in the New Thought movement were sceptical, challenging the compatibility of New Age and New Thought perspectives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199226–27_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199226–27-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> During these decades, Findhorn had become a site of <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a> for many New Agers, and greatly expanded in size as people joined the community, with workshops and conferences being held there that brought together New Age thinkers from across the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike200429_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike200429-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Age_Shrine_Glastonbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_167848.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A new age shrine." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/New_Age_Shrine_Glastonbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_167848.jpg/220px-New_Age_Shrine_Glastonbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_167848.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/New_Age_Shrine_Glastonbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_167848.jpg/330px-New_Age_Shrine_Glastonbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_167848.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/New_Age_Shrine_Glastonbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_167848.jpg/440px-New_Age_Shrine_Glastonbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_167848.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Age_Shrine_Glastonbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_167848.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Age shrine in <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury" title="Glastonbury">Glastonbury</a>, England</div></div></div>
<p>Several key events occurred, which raised public awareness of the New Age subculture: publication of <a href="/wiki/Linda_Goodman" title="Linda Goodman">Linda Goodman</a>'s best-selling astrology books <i>Sun Signs</i> (1968) and <i>Love Signs</i> (1978); the release of <a href="/wiki/Shirley_MacLaine" title="Shirley MacLaine">Shirley MacLaine</a>'s book <i><a href="/wiki/Out_on_a_Limb_(Shirley_MacLaine_book)" class="mw-redirect" title="Out on a Limb (Shirley MacLaine book)">Out on a Limb</a></i> (1983), later adapted into a television mini-series with the same name (1987); and the "<a href="/wiki/Harmonic_Convergence" title="Harmonic Convergence">Harmonic Convergence</a>" <a href="/wiki/Planetary_alignment" class="mw-redirect" title="Planetary alignment">planetary alignment</a> on August 16 and 17, 1987,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> organized by <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Arg%C3%BCelles" title="José Argüelles">José Argüelles</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sedona,_Arizona" title="Sedona, Arizona">Sedona, Arizona</a>. The Convergence attracted more people to the movement than any other single event.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisMelton1992ix_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisMelton1992ix-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Heelas suggested that the movement was influenced by the "enterprise culture" encouraged by the U.S. and U.K. governments during the 1980s onward, with its emphasis on initiative and self-reliance resonating with any New Age ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996168_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996168-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The claims of channelers <a href="/wiki/Jane_Roberts" title="Jane Roberts">Jane Roberts</a> (<a href="/wiki/Seth_Material" title="Seth Material">Seth Material</a>), Helen Schucman (<i><a href="/wiki/A_Course_in_Miracles" title="A Course in Miracles">A Course in Miracles</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/J._Z._Knight" title="J. Z. Knight">J. Z. Knight</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ramtha" class="mw-redirect" title="Ramtha">Ramtha</a>), <a href="/wiki/Neale_Donald_Walsch" title="Neale Donald Walsch">Neale Donald Walsch</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Conversations_with_God" title="Conversations with God">Conversations with God</a></i>) (note that Walsch denies being a "channeler" and his books make it obvious that he is not one, though the text emerged through a dialogue with a deeper part of himself in a process comparable to <a href="/wiki/Automatic_writing" title="Automatic writing">automatic writing</a>) contributed to the movement's growth.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The first significant exponent of the New Age movement in the U.S. has been cited as <a href="/wiki/Ram_Dass" title="Ram Dass">Ram Dass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle199566–67York199535_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle199566–67York199535-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Core works in the propagating New Age ideas included Jane Roberts's Seth series, published from 1972 onward,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike200416_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike200416-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Helen Schucman's 1975 publication <i>A Course in Miracles</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199535–36Pike200416_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199535–36Pike200416-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/James_Redfield" title="James Redfield">James Redfield</a>'s 1993 work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Celestine_Prophecy" title="The Celestine Prophecy">The Celestine Prophecy</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a127Pike200416_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a127Pike200416-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> A variety of these books were <a href="/wiki/Best_seller" class="mw-redirect" title="Best seller">best sellers</a>, with the Seth book series for instance selling over a million copies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike200416_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike200416-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Supplementing these books were videos, audiotapes, compact discs and websites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike200417_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike200417-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The development of the internet in particular further popularized New Age ideas and made them more widely accessible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike200418_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike200418-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>New Age ideas influenced the development of <a href="/wiki/Rave" title="Rave">rave</a> culture in the late 1980s and 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge2004168_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge2004168-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In Britain during the 1980s, the term <i><a href="/wiki/New_Age_Travellers" class="mw-redirect" title="New Age Travellers">New Age Travellers</a></i> came into use,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a129_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a129-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> although York characterised this term as "a misnomer created by the media".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001370_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001370-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> These New Age Travellers had little to do with the New Age as the term was used more widely,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides20078_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides20078-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> with scholar of religion Daren Kemp observing that "New Age spirituality is not an essential part of New Age Traveller culture, although there are similarities between the two worldviews".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp200434_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp200434-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The term <i>New Age</i> came to be used increasingly widely by the popular media in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a129_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a129-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Decline_or_transformation.3F:_1990.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_or_transformation?:_1990–present">Decline or transformation?: 1990–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Decline or transformation?: 1990–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the late 1980s, some publishers dropped the term <i>New Age</i> as a marketing device.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199335_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199335-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In 1994, the scholar of religion <a href="/wiki/Gordon_J._Melton" class="mw-redirect" title="Gordon J. Melton">Gordon J. Melton</a> presented a conference paper in which he argued that, given that he knew of nobody describing their practices as "New Age" anymore, the New Age had died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp2004178_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp2004178-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In 2001, Hammer observed that the term <i>New Age</i> had increasingly been rejected as either pejorative or meaningless by individuals within the Western cultic milieu.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200174_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200174-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> He also noted that within this milieu it was not being replaced by any alternative, and that as such a sense of collective identity was being lost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200174_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200174-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other scholars disagreed with Melton's idea; in 2004 Daren Kemp stated that "New Age is still very much alive".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp2004179_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp2004179-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Hammer himself stated that "the New Age <i>movement</i> may be on the wane, but the wider New Age <i>religiosity</i>... shows no sign of disappearing".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200175_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200175-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> MacKian suggested that the New Age "movement" had been replaced by a wider "New Age sentiment" which had come to pervade "the socio-cultural landscape" of Western countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian20127_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian20127-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Its diffusion into the mainstream may have been influenced by the adoption of New Age concepts by high-profile figures: U.S. First Lady <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Reagan" title="Nancy Reagan">Nancy Reagan</a> consulted an astrologer, British <a href="/wiki/Diana,_Princess_of_Wales" title="Diana, Princess of Wales">Princess Diana</a> visited spirit mediums, and Norwegian Princess <a href="/wiki/Princess_M%C3%A4rtha_Louise_of_Norway" title="Princess Märtha Louise of Norway">Märtha Louise</a> established a school devoted to communicating with angels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012146_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012146-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> 
New Age shops continued to operate, although many have been remarketed as "Mind, Body, Spirit".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides200717_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides200717-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, the scholar of religion <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Urban" title="Hugh Urban">Hugh Urban</a> argued that New Age spirituality is growing in the United States and can be expected to become more visible: "According to many recent surveys of religious affiliation, the 'spiritual but not religious' category is one of the fastest-growing trends in American culture, so the New Age attitude of spiritual individualism and eclecticism may well be an increasingly visible one in the decades to come".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUrban201511_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrban201511-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Australian scholar Paul J. Farrelly, in his 2017 doctoral dissertation at <a href="/wiki/Australian_National_University" title="Australian National University">Australian National University</a>, argued that, while the New Age may become less popular in the West, it is actually booming in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, where it is regarded as something comparatively new, and is being exported from Taiwan to Mainland <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, while it is more or less tolerated by the authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIntrovigne2018_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIntrovigne2018-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs_and_practices">Beliefs and practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Beliefs and practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_but_not_religious" title="Spiritual but not religious">Spiritual but not religious</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_New_Age_topics" title="List of New Age topics">List of New Age topics</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eclecticism_and_self-spirituality">Eclecticism and self-spirituality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Eclecticism and self-spirituality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The New Age places strong emphasis on the idea that the individual and their own experiences are the primary source of authority on spiritual matters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996201–202Heelas199621–23Pike200426_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996201–202Heelas199621–23Pike200426-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> It exhibits what Heelas termed "unmediated individualism",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199621–23_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199621–23-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> and reflects a world-view that is "radically democratic".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiordan1992124_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiordan1992124-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> It places an emphasis on the freedom and autonomy of the individual.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199626–27MacKian2012119_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199626–27MacKian2012119-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> This emphasis has led to ethical disagreements; some New Agers believe helping others is beneficial, although another view is that doing so encourages dependency and conflicts with a reliance on the self.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199625_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199625-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, within the New Age, there are differences in the role accorded to voices of authority outside of the self.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199634_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199634-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Hammer stated that "a belief in the existence of a core or true Self" is a "recurring theme" in New Age texts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200111_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200111-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The concept of "<a href="/wiki/Personal_growth" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal growth">personal growth</a>" is also greatly emphasised among New Agers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199646Sutcliffe2003a188_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199646Sutcliffe2003a188-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> while Heelas noted that "for participants spirituality <i>is</i> life-itself".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas200627_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas200627-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>New Age religiosity is typified by its eclecticism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer2001163_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer2001163-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Generally believing that there is no one true way to pursue spirituality,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012118–119_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012118–119-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> New Agers develop their own worldview "by combining bits and pieces to form their own individual mix",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200128_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200128-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> seeking what Drury called "a spirituality without borders or confining dogmas".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrury20048_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrury20048-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The anthropologist David J. Hess noted that in his experience, a common attitude among New Agers was that "any alternative spiritual path is good because it is spiritual and alternative".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199336_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199336-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> This approach that has generated a common jibe that New Age represents "supermarket spirituality".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge200432_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge200432-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> York suggested that this eclecticism stemmed from the New Age's origins within <a href="/wiki/Late_modernity" title="Late modernity">late modern</a> capitalism, with New Agers subscribing to a belief in a free market of spiritual ideas as a parallel to a free market in economics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001367_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001367-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of its eclecticism, the New Age draws ideas from many different cultural and spiritual traditions from across the world, often legitimising this approach by reference to "a very vague claim" about underlying global unity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer2001139_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer2001139-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Certain societies are more usually chosen over others;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer2001199Kemp200449_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer2001199Kemp200449-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> examples include the ancient Celts, ancient Egyptians, the <a href="/wiki/Essenes" title="Essenes">Essenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantis" title="Atlantis">Atlanteans</a>, and ancient extraterrestrials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp200447_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp200447-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> As noted by Hammer: "to put it bluntly, no significant spokespersons within the New Age community claim to represent ancient Albanian wisdom, simply because beliefs regarding ancient Albanians are not part of our cultural stereotypes".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer2001199_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer2001199-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> According to Hess, these ancient or foreign societies represent an exotic "Other" for New Agers, who are predominantly white Westerners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199348_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199348-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Theology.2C_cosmogony.2C_and_cosmology"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology,_cosmogony,_and_cosmology">Theology, cosmogony, and cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Theology, cosmogony, and cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Snoqualmie_Moondance_meditation_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Snoqualmie_Moondance_meditation_02.jpg/220px-Snoqualmie_Moondance_meditation_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="328" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Snoqualmie_Moondance_meditation_02.jpg/330px-Snoqualmie_Moondance_meditation_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Snoqualmie_Moondance_meditation_02.jpg/440px-Snoqualmie_Moondance_meditation_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2372" data-file-height="3534" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Snoqualmie_Moondance_meditation_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Age meditation group at the Snoqualmie Moondance festival, 1992</div></div></div>
<p>A belief in <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divinity</a> is integral to New Age ideas, although understandings of this divinity vary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996182–183_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996182–183-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> New Age <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> exhibits an inclusive and universalistic approach that accepts all personal perspectives on the divine as equally valid.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996185_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996185-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> This intentional vagueness as to the nature of divinity also reflects the New Age idea that divinity cannot be comprehended by the human mind or language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996186_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996186-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> New Age literature nevertheless displays recurring traits in its depiction of the divine: the first is the idea that it is <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holistic</a>, thus frequently being described with such terms as an "Ocean of Oneness", "Infinite Spirit", "Primal Stream", "One Essence", and "Universal Principle".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996186_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996186-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> A second trait is the characterisation of divinity as "Mind", "Consciousness", and "Intelligence",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996186–187_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996186–187-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> while a third is the description of divinity as a form of "<a href="/wiki/Energy_(esotericism)" title="Energy (esotericism)">energy</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996187_145-0" class="reference"><a
