href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996187-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> A fourth trait is the characterisation of divinity as a "life force", the essence of which is creativity, while a fifth is the concept that divinity consists of <a href="/wiki/Love" title="Love">love</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996187–188_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996187–188-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most New Age groups believe in an Ultimate Source from which all things originate, which is usually conflated with the divine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996120_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996120-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Various <a href="/wiki/Creation_myths" class="mw-redirect" title="Creation myths">creation myths</a> have been articulated in New Age publications outlining how this Ultimate Source created the universe and everything in it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996122–123_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996122–123-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, some New Agers emphasise the idea of a universal inter-relatedness that is not always emanating from a single source.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996128_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996128-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The New Age worldview emphasises holism and the idea that everything in existence is intricately connected as part of a single whole,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996119Drury200411_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996119Drury200411-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> in doing so rejecting both the <a href="/wiki/Dualistic_cosmology" title="Dualistic cosmology">dualism</a> of Judeo-Christian thought and the <a href="/wiki/Reductionism" title="Reductionism">reductionism</a> of Cartesian science.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996119_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996119-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> A number of New Agers have linked this holistic interpretation of the universe to the <a href="/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis" title="Gaia hypothesis">Gaia hypothesis</a> of <a href="/wiki/James_Lovelock" title="James Lovelock">James Lovelock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike200423_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike200423-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The idea of holistic divinity results in a common New Age belief that humans themselves are divine in essence, a concept described using such terms as "droplet of divinity", "inner Godhead", and "divine self".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996204_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996204-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Influenced by Theosophical and Anthroposophical ideas regarding '<a href="/wiki/Subtle_bodies" class="mw-redirect" title="Subtle bodies">subtle bodies</a>',<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996222–223_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996222–223-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> a common New Age idea holds to the existence of a "Higher Self" that is a part of the human but connects with the divine essence of the universe, and which can advise the human mind through <a href="/wiki/Intuition" title="Intuition">intuition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996211–212_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996211–212-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cosmogonical creation stories are common in New Age sources,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996304_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996304-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> with these accounts reflecting the movement's holistic framework by describing an original, primal oneness from which all things in the universe emanated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996305_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996305-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> An additional common theme is that human souls – once living in a spiritual world – then descended into a world of matter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996307–308_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996307–308-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
The New Age movement typically views the material universe as a meaningful illusion, which humans should try to use constructively rather than focus on escaping into other spiritual realms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996115_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996115-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> This physical world is hence seen as "a domain for learning and growth" after which the human soul might pass on to higher levels of existence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996116–117_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996116–117-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> There is thus a widespread belief that reality is engaged in an ongoing process of evolution; rather than <a href="/wiki/Darwinian_evolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Darwinian evolution">Darwinian evolution</a>, this is typically seen as either a teleological evolution which assumes a process headed to a specific goal, or an open-ended, creative evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996158–160_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996158–160-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spirit_and_channeling">Spirit and channeling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Spirit and channeling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"In the flood of channeled material which has been published or delivered to "live" audiences in the last two decades, there is much indeed that is trivial, contradictory, and confusing. The authors of much of this material make claims that, while not necessarily untrue or fraudulent, are difficult or impossible for the reader to verify. A number of other channeled documents address issues more immediately relevant to the human condition. The best of these writings are not only coherent and plausible, but eloquently persuasive and sometimes disarmingly moving."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Academic Suzanne Riordan, 1992<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiordan1992107_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiordan1992107-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>MacKian argued that a central, but often overlooked, element of the phenomenon was an emphasis on "<a href="/wiki/Spirit" title="Spirit">spirit</a>", and in particular participants' desire for a relationship with spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian201251,_117_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian201251,_117-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Many practitioners in her UK-focused study described themselves as "workers <i>for</i> spirit", expressing the desire to help people learn about spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012145_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012145-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> They understood various material signs as marking the presence of spirit, for instance the unexpected appearance of a feather.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012142–143_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012142–143-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> New Agers often call upon this spirit to assist them in everyday situations, for instance to ease the traffic flow on their way to work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian201284,_143_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian201284,_143-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>New Age literature often refers to benevolent non-human spirit-beings who are interested in humanity's spiritual development; these are variously referred to as angels, <a href="/wiki/Guardian_angel" title="Guardian angel">guardian angels</a>, personal guides, masters, teachers, and contacts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996197,_198_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996197,_198-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> New Age angelology is nevertheless unsystematic, reflecting the idiosyncrasies of individual authors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996198_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996198-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The figure of Jesus Christ is often mentioned within New Age literature as a mediating principle between divinity and humanity, as well as an exemplar of a spiritually advanced human being.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996189–190_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996189–190-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although not present in every New Age group,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiordan1992105_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiordan1992105-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> a core belief within the milieu is in <a href="/wiki/Mediumship" title="Mediumship">channeling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199221Hanegraaff199623Pike200424_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199221Hanegraaff199623Pike200424-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> This is the idea that humans beings, sometimes (although not always) in a state of <a href="/wiki/Trance" title="Trance">trance</a>, can act "as a channel of information from sources other than their normal selves".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199623–24_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199623–24-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> These sources are varyingly described as being <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gods" class="mw-redirect" title="Gods">gods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goddesses" class="mw-redirect" title="Goddesses">goddesses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ascended_masters" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascended masters">ascended masters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spirit_guide" title="Spirit guide">spirit guides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrials</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deva_(New_Age)" title="Deva (New Age)">devas</a>, historical figures, the <a href="/wiki/Collective_unconscious" title="Collective unconscious">collective unconscious</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elemental" title="Elemental">elementals</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Nature_spirit" class="mw-redirect" title="Nature spirit">nature spirits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199623–24_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199623–24-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Hanegraaff described channeling as a form of "articulated revelation",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199624_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199624-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> and identified four forms: trance channeling, automatisms, clairaudient channeling, and open channeling.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199627–28_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199627–28-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prominent examples of New Age channeling include Jane Roberts' claims that she was contacted by an entity called Seth, and Helen Schucman's claims to have channeled Jesus Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199222Riordan1992108–110Pike200428_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199222Riordan1992108–110Pike200428-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> The academic Suzanne Riordan examined a variety of these New Age channeled messages, noting that they typically "echoed each other in tone and content", offering an analysis of the human condition and giving instructions or advice for how humanity can discover its true destiny.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiordan1992110_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiordan1992110-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
For many New Agers, these channeled messages rival the scriptures of the main world religions as sources of spiritual authority,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiordan1992108_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiordan1992108-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> although often New Agers describe historical religious revelations as forms of "channeling" as well, thus attempting to legitimate and authenticate their own contemporary practices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199626–27_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199626–27-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Although the concept of channeling from discarnate spirit entities has links to <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">Spiritualism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychical_research" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychical research">psychical research</a>, the New Age does not feature Spiritualism's emphasis on proving the existence of life after death, nor psychical research's focus of testing mediums for consistency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199223_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199223-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astrological_cycles_and_the_Age_of_Aquarius">Astrological cycles and the Age of Aquarius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Astrological cycles and the Age of Aquarius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>New Age thought typically envisions the world as developing through cosmological cycles that can be identified <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrologically</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996102,_302_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996102,_302-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> It adopts this concept from Theosophy, although often presents it in a looser and more eclectic way than is found in Theosophical teaching.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996303_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996303-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> New Age literature often claims that humanity once lived in an age of spiritual wisdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996302_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996302-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> In the writings of New Agers like Edgar Cayce, the ancient period of spiritual wisdom is associated with concepts of supremely-advanced societies living on <a href="/wiki/Lost_lands" class="mw-redirect" title="Lost lands">lost continents</a> such as Atlantis, <a href="/wiki/Lemuria_(continent)" title="Lemuria (continent)">Lemuria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mu_(lost_continent)" title="Mu (lost continent)">Mu</a>, as well as the idea that ancient societies like those of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> were far more technologically advanced than modern scholarship accepts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996309–314_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996309–314-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
New Age literature often posits that the ancient period of spiritual wisdom gave way to an age of spiritual decline, sometimes termed the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Pisces" class="mw-redirect" title="Age of Pisces">Age of Pisces</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996302_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996302-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Although characterised as being a negative period for humanity, New Age literature views the Age of Pisces as an important learning experience for the species.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996320_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996320-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> 
Hanegraaff stated that New Age perceptions of history were "extremely sketchy" in their use of description,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996320_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996320-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> reflecting little interest in <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a> and conflating history with <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">myth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996324_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996324-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> He also noted that they were highly <a href="/wiki/Ethnocentrism" title="Ethnocentrism">ethnocentric</a> in placing <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western civilization</a> at the centre of historical development.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996303_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996303-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venice_ast_sm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Venice_ast_sm.jpg/220px-Venice_ast_sm.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Venice_ast_sm.jpg/330px-Venice_ast_sm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/12/Venice_ast_sm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Venice_ast_sm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astrological ideas hold a central place in the New Age</div></div></div>
<p>A common belief among the New Age is that humanity has entered, or is coming to enter, a new period known as the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Aquarius" title="Age of Aquarius">Age of Aquarius</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199694_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199694-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> which Melton has characterised as a "New Age of love, joy, peace, abundance, and harmony[...] the Golden Age heretofore only dreamed about."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199219_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199219-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In accepting this belief in a coming new age, the milieu has been described as "highly positive, celebratory, [and] utopian",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199628_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199628-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> and has also been cited as an <a href="/wiki/Apocalypticism" title="Apocalypticism">apocalyptic movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199224_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199224-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Opinions about the nature of the coming Age of Aquarius differ among New Agers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996333_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996333-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> There are for instance differences in belief about its commencement; New Age author David Spangler claimed that it began in 1967,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996334–335_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996334–335-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> others placed its beginning with the Harmonic Convergence of 1987,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996335_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996335-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> author José Argüelles predicted its start <a href="/wiki/2012_phenomenon" title="2012 phenomenon">in 2012</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199372_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199372-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> and some believe that it will not begin until several centuries into the third millennium.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996335–336_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996335–336-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are also differences in how this new age is envisioned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996336_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996336-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Those adhering to what Hanegraaff termed the "moderate" perspective believed that it would be marked by an improvement to current society, which affected both New Age concerns—through the convergence of science and mysticism and the global embrace of alternative medicine—to more general concerns, including an end to violence, crime and war, a healthier environment, and international co-operation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996339–340_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996339–340-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Other New Agers adopt a fully utopian vision, believing that the world will be wholly transformed into an "Age of Light", with humans evolving into totally spiritual beings and experiencing unlimited love, bliss, and happiness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996341—343_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996341—343-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Rather than conceiving of the Age of Aquarius as an indefinite period, many believe that it would last for around two thousand years before being replaced by a further age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996102_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996102-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are various beliefs within the milieu as to how this new age will come about, but most emphasise the idea that it will be established through <a href="/wiki/Human_agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Human agency">human agency</a>; others assert that it will be established with the aid of non-human forces such as spirits or extraterrestrials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199674_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199674-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Ferguson for instance claimed that there was a vanguard of humans known as the "Aquarian conspiracy" who were helping to bring the Age of Aquarius forth through their actions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199370_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199370-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Participants in the New Age typically express the view that their own spiritual actions are helping to bring about the Age of Aquarius,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199219Heelas199675–76_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199219Heelas199675–76-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> with writers like Ferguson and Argüelles presenting themselves as prophets ushering forth this future era.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199373_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199373-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Healing_and_alternative_medicine">Healing and alternative medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Healing and alternative medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another recurring element of New Age is an emphasis on healing and <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">alternative medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllwood199260York199537Hanegraaff199642Sutcliffe2003a174_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllwood199260York199537Hanegraaff199642Sutcliffe2003a174-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Butler_2007_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butler_2007-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The general New Age ethos is that health is the natural state for the human being and that illness is a disruption of that natural balance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199646–47_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199646–47-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Hence, New Age therapies seek to heal "<a href="/wiki/Illness" class="mw-redirect" title="Illness">illness</a>" as a general concept that includes physical, mental, and spiritual aspects; in doing so it critiques mainstream Western medicine for simply attempting to cure <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>, and thus has an affinity with most forms of <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbanese199281–82Hanegraaff199642–43Sutcliffe2003a176_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbanese199281–82Hanegraaff199642–43Sutcliffe2003a176-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Its focus of self-spirituality has led to the emphasis of self-healing,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199682–87Sutcliffe2003a176,_178MacKian2012160_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199682–87Sutcliffe2003a176,_178MacKian2012160-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> although also present are ideas on healing both others and the Earth itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199682–87MacKian2012160_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199682–87MacKian2012160-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Siete_chakras.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Siete_chakras.jpg/170px-Siete_chakras.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Siete_chakras.jpg/255px-Siete_chakras.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Siete_chakras.jpg/340px-Siete_chakras.jpg 2x" data-file-width="643" data-file-height="864" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Siete_chakras.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Reiki" title="Reiki">Reiki</a> is one of the alternative therapies commonly found in the New Age movement.</div></div></div>
<p>The healing elements of the movement are difficult to classify given that a variety of terms are used, with some New Age authors using different terms to refer to the same trends, while others use the same term to refer to different things.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199648_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199648-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> However, Hanegraaff developed a set of categories into which the forms of New Age healing could be roughly categorised. The first of these was the <a href="/wiki/Human_Potential_Movement" title="Human Potential Movement">Human Potential Movement</a>, which argues that contemporary Western society suppresses much human potential, and accordingly professes to offer a path through which individuals can access those parts of themselves that they have alienated and suppressed, thus enabling them to reach their full potential and live a meaningful life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199648–49_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199648–49-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Hanegraaff described <a href="/wiki/Transpersonal_psychology" title="Transpersonal psychology">transpersonal psychology</a> as the "theoretical wing" of this Human Potential Movement; in contrast to other schools of psychological thought, transpersonal psychology takes religious and mystical experiences seriously by exploring the uses of <a href="/wiki/Altered_states_of_consciousness" class="mw-redirect" title="Altered states of consciousness">altered states of consciousness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199650–51_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199650–51-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Closely connected to this is the <a href="/wiki/Neoshamanism" title="Neoshamanism">shamanic consciousness</a> current, which argues that the <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shaman</a> was a specialist in altered states of consciousness and seeks to adopt and imitate traditional shamanic techniques as a form of personal healing and growth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199652_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199652-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hanegraaff identified the second main healing current in the New Age movement as being <a href="/wiki/Holistic_health" class="mw-redirect" title="Holistic health">holistic health</a>. This emerged in the 1970s out of the <a href="/wiki/Free_clinic" title="Free clinic">free clinic</a> movement of the 1960s, and has various connections with the Human Potential Movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199653–54_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199653–54-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> It emphasises the idea that the human individual is a holistic, interdependent relationship between mind, body, and spirit, and that healing is a process in which an individual becomes whole by integrating with the powers of the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199654_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199654-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> A very wide array of methods are utilised within the holistic health movement, with some of the most common including <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a>, reiki, <a href="/wiki/Biofeedback" title="Biofeedback">biofeedback</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiropractic" title="Chiropractic">chiropractic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kinesiology" title="Kinesiology">kinesiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aromatherapy" title="Aromatherapy">aromatherapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iridology" title="Iridology">iridology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massage" title="Massage">massage</a> and other forms of <a href="/wiki/Bodywork_(alternative_medicine)" title="Bodywork (alternative medicine)">bodywork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">visualisation</a>, nutritional therapy, <a href="/wiki/Psychic_healing" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychic healing">psychic healing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbal_medicine" title="Herbal medicine">herbal medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crystal_healing" title="Crystal healing">healing using crystals</a>, metals, music, <a href="/wiki/Chromotherapy" title="Chromotherapy">chromotherapy</a>, and reincarnation therapy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199537Hanegraaff199654–55Kemp200430_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199537Hanegraaff199654–55Kemp200430-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> The use of crystal healing has become a particularly prominent visual trope within the New Age;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbanese199279_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbanese199279-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> this practice was not common in esotericism prior to their adoption in the New Age milieu.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer2001162_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer2001162-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>
The mainstreaming of the Holistic Health movement in the UK is discussed by Maria Tighe. The inter-relation of holistic health with the New Age movement is illustrated in Jenny Butler's ethnographic description of "Angel therapy" in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-Butler_2007_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butler_2007-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Age_science">New Age science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: New Age science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"The New Age is essentially about the search for spiritual and philosophical perspectives that will help transform humanity and the world. New Agers are willing to absorb wisdom teachings wherever they can find them, whether from an Indian guru, a renegade Christian priest, an itinerant Buddhist monk, an experiential psychotherapist or a Native American shaman. They are eager to explore their own inner potential with a view to becoming part of a broader process of social transformation. Their journey is towards totality of being."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— New Ager <a href="/wiki/Nevill_Drury" title="Nevill Drury">Nevill Drury</a>, 2004<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrury200411_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrury200411-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>According to Drury, the New Age attempts to create "a worldview that includes both science and spirituality",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrury200410_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrury200410-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> while Hess noted how New Agers have "a penchant for bringing together the technical and the spiritual, the scientific and the religious".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess19934_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess19934-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
Although New Agers typically reject <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>, and the academic establishment, they employ terminology and concepts borrowed from science and particularly from the <a href="/wiki/Physics_beyond_the_Standard_Model" title="Physics beyond the Standard Model">New Physics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas19965Hanegraaff199662_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas19965Hanegraaff199662-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, a number of prominent influences on New Age, such as <a href="/wiki/David_Bohm" title="David Bohm">David Bohm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ilya_Prigogine" title="Ilya Prigogine">Ilya Prigogine</a>, had backgrounds as professional scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199663_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199663-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Hanegraaff identified "New Age science" as a form of <i><a href="/wiki/Naturphilosophie" title="Naturphilosophie">Naturphilosophie</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199664_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199664-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In this, the milieu is interested in developing unified world views to discover the nature of the divine and establish a scientific basis for religious belief.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199663_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199663-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Figures in the New Age movement—most notably <a href="/wiki/Fritjof_Capra" title="Fritjof Capra">Fritjof Capra</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tao_of_Physics" title="The Tao of Physics">The Tao of Physics</a></i> (1975) and <a href="/wiki/Gary_Zukav" title="Gary Zukav">Gary Zukav</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dancing_Wu_Li_Masters" title="The Dancing Wu Li Masters">The Dancing Wu Li Masters</a></i> (1979)—have drawn parallels between theories in the New Physics and traditional forms of <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a>, thus arguing that ancient religious ideas are now being proven by contemporary science.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996128–129_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996128–129-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Many New Agers have adopted James Lovelock's Gaia hypothesis that the Earth acts akin to a single living organism, although have expanded this idea to include the idea that the Earth has consciousness and intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996155–156Heelas199686_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996155–156Heelas199686-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite New Agers' appeals to science, most of the academic and scientific establishments dismiss "New Age science" as <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-science" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudo-science">pseudo-science</a>, or at best existing in part on the fringes of genuine scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199311Hanegraaff199662_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199311Hanegraaff199662-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> This is an attitude also shared by many active in the field of <a href="/wiki/Parapsychology" title="Parapsychology">parapsychology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199311_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199311-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> In turn, New Agers often accuse the scientific establishment of pursuing a dogmatic and outmoded approach to scientific enquiry,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199368_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199368-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> believing that their own understandings of the universe will replace those of the academic establishment in a <a href="/wiki/Paradigm_shift" title="Paradigm shift">paradigm shift</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas19965Hanegraaff199662_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas19965Hanegraaff199662-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics_and_afterlife">Ethics and afterlife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ethics and afterlife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no ethical cohesion within the New Age phenomenon,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge200435_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge200435-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> although Hanegraaff argued that the central ethical tenet of the New Age is to cultivate one's own divine potential.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996281_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996281-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Given that the movement's holistic interpretation of the universe prohibits a belief in a dualistic <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">good and evil</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996277_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996277-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> negative events that happen are interpreted not as the result of evil but as lessons designed to teach an individual and enable them to advance spiritually.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996236–237,_278_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996236–237,_278-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
It rejects the Christian emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">sin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guilt_(emotion)" title="Guilt (emotion)">guilt</a>, believing that these generate fear and thus negativity, which then hinder spiritual evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996294–295_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996294–295-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> It also typically criticises the blaming and judging of others for their actions, believing that if an individual adopts these negative attitudes it harms their own spiritual evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996293_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996293-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Instead the movement emphasizes positive thinking, although beliefs regarding the power behind such thoughts vary within New Age literature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996240_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996240-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Common New Age examples of how to generate such positive thinking include the repeated recitation of mantras and statements carrying positive messages,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996240—241_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996240—241-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> and the visualisation of a white light.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996242_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996242-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Hanegraaff, the question of death and afterlife is not a "pressing problem requiring an answer" in the New Age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996258_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996258-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> A belief in <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a> is very common, where it often viewed as being part of an individual's progressive spiritual evolution toward realisation of their own divinity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996262York2001364MacKian2012109_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996262York2001364MacKian2012109-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> In New Age literature, the reality of reincarnation is usually treated as self-evident, with no explanation as to why practitioners embrace this afterlife belief over others,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996263_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996263-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> although New Agers endorse it in the belief that it ensures cosmic justice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996264_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996264-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Many New Agers believe in <a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a>, treating it as a law of cause and effect that assures cosmic balance, although in some cases they stress that it is not a system that enforces punishment for past actions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996286_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996286-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>
In much New Age literature on reincarnation, it is claimed that part of the human soul, that which carries the personality, perishes with the death of the body, while the Higher Self – that which connects with divinity – survives in order to be reborn into another body.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996266_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996266-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> It is believed that the Higher Self chooses the body and circumstances into which it will be born, in order to use it as a vessel through which to learn new lessons and thus advance its own spiritual evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996262_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996262-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Prominent New Age writers like <a href="/wiki/Shakti_Gawain" title="Shakti Gawain">Shakti Gawain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louise_Hay" title="Louise Hay">Louise Hay</a> therefore express the view that humans are responsible for the events that happen to them during their life, an idea that many New Agers regard as empowering.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996234_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996234-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> At times, past life regression are employed within the New Age in order to reveal a Higher Soul's previous incarnations, usually with an explicit healing purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996271–275_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996271–275-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Some practitioners espouse the idea of a "soul group" or "soul family", a group of connected souls who reincarnate together as family of friendship units.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012109_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012109-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> Rather than reincarnation, another afterlife belief found among New Agers holds that an individual's soul returns to a "universal energy" on bodily death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012109_246-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012109-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">By the early twenty-first century... [the New Age phenomenon] has an almost entirely white, middle-class demography largely made up of professional, managerial, arts, and entrepreneurial occupations.</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Religious studies scholar Steven J. Sutcliffe<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a223_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a223-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>In the mid-1990s, the New Age was found primarily in the United States and Canada, Western Europe, and Australia and New Zealand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199542_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199542-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> The fact that most individuals engaging in New Age activity do not describe themselves as "New Agers" renders it difficult to determine the total number of practitioners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis19922_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis19922-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Heelas highlighted the range of attempts to establish the number of New Age participants in the U.S. during this period, noting that estimates ranged from 20,000 to 6 million; he believed that the higher ranges of these estimates were greatly inflated by, for instance, an erroneous assumption that all Americans who believed in reincarnation were part of the New Age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996112_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996112-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> He nevertheless suggested that over 10 million people in the U.S. had had some contact with New Age practices or ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996120_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996120-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Between 2000 and 2002, Heelas and Woodhead conducted research into the New Age in the English town of <a href="/wiki/Kendal" title="Kendal">Kendal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>; they found 600 people actively attended New Age activities on a weekly basis, representing 1.6% of the town's population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200545_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200545-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> From this, they extrapolated that around 900,000 Britons regularly took part in New Age activities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200553_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200553-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, Heelas stated that New Age practices had grown to such an extent that they were "increasingly rivalling the sway of Christianity in western settings".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas20064_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas20064-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sociological investigation indicates that certain sectors of society are more likely to engage in New Age practices than others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a223–224_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a223–224-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> In the United States, the first people to embrace the New Age belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Baby_boomer" class="mw-redirect" title="Baby boomer">baby boomer</a> generation, those born between 1946 and 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown199290_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown199290-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sutcliffe noted that although most influential New Age figureheads were male,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a220–221_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a220–221-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> approximately two-thirds of its participants were female.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a220_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a220-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> Heelas and Woodhead's Kendal Project found that of those regularly attending New Age activities in the town, 80% were female, while 78% of those running such activities were female.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200594_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200594-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> They attributed this female dominance to "deeply entrenched cultural values and divisions of labour" in Western society, according to which women were accorded greater responsibility for the well-being of others, thus making New Age practices more attractive to them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005100_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005100-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> They suggested that men were less attracted to New Age activities because they were hampered by a "masculinist ideal of autonomy and self-sufficiency" which discouraged them from seeking the assistance of others for their inner development.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005106–107_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005106–107-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of New Agers are from the middle and upper-middle classes of Western society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996121Partridge200453_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996121Partridge200453-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Heelas and Woodhead found that of the active Kendal New Agers, 57% had a university or college degree.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200593_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200593-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Their Kendal Project also determined that 73% of active New Agers were aged over 45, and 55% were aged between 40 and 59; it also determined that many got involved while middle-aged.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005107_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005107-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Comparatively few were either young or elderly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005127_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005127-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> Heelas and Woodhead suggested that the dominance of middle-aged people, particularly women, was because at this stage of life they had greater time to devote to their own inner development, with their time previously having been dominated by raising children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005108_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005108-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> They also suggested that middle-aged people were experiencing more age-related ailments than the young, and thus more keen to pursue New Age activities to improve their health.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005109_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead2005109-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rave_in_the_Henge_2005.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Stonehenge at night, with revellers inside" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Rave_in_the_Henge_2005.jpg/200px-Rave_in_the_Henge_2005.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Rave_in_the_Henge_2005.jpg/300px-Rave_in_the_Henge_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Rave_in_the_Henge_2005.jpg/400px-Rave_in_the_Henge_2005.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="556" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rave_in_the_Henge_2005.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a> is a site visited by New Age pilgrims, as seen in this midsummer rave.</div></div></div>
<p>Heelas added that within the baby boomers, the movement had nevertheless attracted a diverse clientele.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996136_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996136-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> He typified the typical New Ager as someone who was well-educated yet disenchanted with mainstream society, thus arguing that the movement catered to those who believe that <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a> is in crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996137–138_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996137–138-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> He suggested that the movement appealed to many former practitioners of the 1960s counter-culture because while they came to feel that they were unable to change society, they were nonetheless interested in changing the self.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996142_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996142-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> He believed that many individuals had been "culturally primed for what the New Age has to offer",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996172_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996172-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> with the New Age attracting "expressive" people who were already comfortable with the ideals and outlooks of the movement's self-spirituality focus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996161–162_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996161–162-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> It could be particularly appealing because the New Age suited the needs of the individual, whereas traditional religious options that are available primarily catered for the needs of a community.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996173_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996173-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> He believed that although the adoption of New Age beliefs and practices by some fitted the model of <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">religious conversion</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996186_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996186-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> others who adopted some of its practices could not easily be considered to have converted to the religion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996183_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996183-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> Sutcliffe described the "typical" participant in the New Age milieu as being "a religious individualist, mixing and matching cultural resources in an animated spiritual quest".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a200_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a200-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The degree to which individuals are involved in the New Age varies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996117_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996117-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> Heelas argued that those involved could be divided into three broad groups; the first comprised those who were completely dedicated to it and its ideals, often working in professions that furthered those goals. The second consisted of "serious part-timers" who worked in unrelated fields but who nevertheless spent much of their free time involved in movement activities. The third was that of "casual part-timers" who occasionally involved themselves in New Age activities but for whom the movement was not a central aspect of their life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996118–119_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996118–119-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> MacKian instead suggested that involvement could be seen as being layered like an onion; at the core are "consultative" practitioners who devote their life to New Age practices, around that are "serious" practitioners who still invest considerable effort into New Age activities, and on the periphery are "non-practitioner consumers", individuals affected by the general dissemination of New Age ideas but who do not devote themselves more fully to them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian201273–74_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian201273–74-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Many New Age practices have filtered into wider Western society, with a 2000 poll for instance revealing that 39% of the UK population had tried alternative therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge200452_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge200452-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1995, Kyle stated that on the whole, New Agers in the United States preferred the values of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> over those of the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>. He added that most New Agers "soundly rejected" the agenda of former Republican President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b846_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b846-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_communities">Social communities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Social communities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>MacKian suggested that this phenomenon was "an inherently <i>social</i> mode of spirituality", one which cultivated a sense of belonging among its participants and encouraged relations both with other humans and with non-human, otherworldly spirit entities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012136_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012136-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup>
MacKian suggested that these communities "may look very different" from those of traditional religious groups.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012135_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012135-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Online connections were one of the ways that interested individuals met new contacts and established networks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012131_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012131-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_aspects">Commercial aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Commercial aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Age_shop_and_Healing_centre,_St_Albans,_Hertfordshire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="New Age store along stone street with bicycles parked out front and large crystals behind display windows" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/New_Age_shop_and_Healing_centre%2C_St_Albans%2C_Hertfordshire.jpg/330px-New_Age_shop_and_Healing_centre%2C_St_Albans%2C_Hertfordshire.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/New_Age_shop_and_Healing_centre%2C_St_Albans%2C_Hertfordshire.jpg/495px-New_Age_shop_and_Healing_centre%2C_St_Albans%2C_Hertfordshire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/New_Age_shop_and_Healing_centre%2C_St_Albans%2C_Hertfordshire.jpg/660px-New_Age_shop_and_Healing_centre%2C_St_Albans%2C_Hertfordshire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Age_shop_and_Healing_centre,_St_Albans,_Hertfordshire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Isis, a New Age shop named after the <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Ancient Egyptian goddess</a> that was located in St Albans, southern England</div></div></div>
<p>Some New Agers advocate living in a <a href="/wiki/Simple_living" title="Simple living">simple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_living" title="Sustainable living">sustainable</a> manner to reduce humanity's impact on the <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a> of Earth; and they shun <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199674–75_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199674–75-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> The New Age movement has been centered around rebuilding a <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_community" title="Sense of community">sense of community</a> to counter <a href="/wiki/Social_disintegration" title="Social disintegration">social disintegration</a>; this has been attempted through the formation of <a href="/wiki/Intentional_communities" class="mw-redirect" title="Intentional communities">intentional communities</a>, where individuals come together to live and work in a communal lifestyle.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> New Age centres have been set up in various parts of the world, representing an institutionalised form of the movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199540–41_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199540–41-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> Notable examples include the <a href="/wiki/Naropa_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Naropa Institute">Naropa Institute</a> in <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">Boulder, Colorado</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Holly_Hock_Farm&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Holly Hock Farm (page does not exist)">Holly Hock Farm</a> near to <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Wrekin_Trust" title="Wrekin Trust">Wrekin Trust</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Malvern" title="West Malvern">West Malvern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worcestershire" title="Worcestershire">Worcestershire</a>, and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyros_Centre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Skyros Centre (page does not exist)">Skyros Centre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Skyros" title="Skyros">Skyros</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199541_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199541-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> Criticising mainstream Western education as counterproductive to the ethos of the movement, many New Age groups have established their own schools for the education of children, although in other cases such groups have sought to introduce New Age spiritual techniques into pre-existing establishments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199677–78_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199677–78-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bruce argued that in seeking to "denying the validity of externally imposed controls and privileging the divine within", the New Age sought to dismantle pre-existing social order, but that it failed to present anything adequate in its place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199832–33_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199832–33-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> Heelas however cautioned that Bruce had arrived at this conclusion based on "flimsy evidence",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1998258_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1998258-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> and Aldred argued that only a minority of New Agers participate in community-focused activities; instead, she claimed, the majority of New Agers participate mainly through the purchase of books and products targeted at the New Age market, positioning New Age as a primarily consumerist and commercial movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Aldred_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aldred-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fairs_and_festivals">Fairs and festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Fairs and festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>New Age spirituality has led to a wide array of literature on the subject and an active niche market, with books, music, crafts, and services in alternative medicine available at New Age stores, <a href="/wiki/Fair" title="Fair">fairs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Festival" title="Festival">festivals</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
New Age fairs – sometimes known as "Mind, Body, Spirit fairs", "psychic fairs", or "alternative health fairs" – are spaces in which a variety of goods and services are displayed by different vendors, including forms of alternative medicine and esoteric practices such as palmistry or tarot card reading.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a181–183_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a181–183-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> A prominent example is the <a href="/wiki/Mind_Body_Spirit_Festival" title="Mind Body Spirit Festival">Mind Body Spirit Festival</a>, held annually in the United Kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge200465MacKian201224_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge200465MacKian201224-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> at which – the religious studies scholar Christopher Partridge noted – one could encounter "a wide range of beliefs and practices from crystal healing to ... Kirlian photography to psychic art, from angels to past-life therapy, from Theosophy to UFO religion, and from New Age music to the vegetarianism of Suma Chign Hai."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge200465_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge200465-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> Similar festivals are held across Europe and in Australia and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian201224_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian201224-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches_to_financial_prosperity_and_business">Approaches to financial prosperity and business</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Approaches to financial prosperity and business">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A number of New Age proponents have emphasised the use of spiritual techniques as a tool for attaining financial prosperity, thus moving the movement away from its counter-cultural origins.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199660–62_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199660–62-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> Commenting on this "New Age capitalism", Hess observed that it was largely small-scale and entrepreneurial, focused around small companies run by members of the <a href="/wiki/Petty_bourgeoisie" class="mw-redirect" title="Petty bourgeoisie">petty bourgeoisie</a>, rather than being dominated by large scale multinational corporations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHess199338_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHess199338-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> The links between New Age and commercial products have resulted in the accusation that New Age itself is little more than a manifestation of <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas20066_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas20066-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> This idea is generally rejected by New Age participants, who often reject any link between their practices and consumerist activities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas200685_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas200685-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Embracing this attitude, various books have been published espousing such an ethos, established New Age centres have held spiritual retreats and classes aimed specifically at business people, and New Age groups have developed specialised training for businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas199662–65_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas199662–65-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> During the 1980s, many prominent U.S. corporations—among them <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">AT&amp;T</a>, and <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>—embraced New Age seminars, hoping that they could increase productivity and efficiency among their work force,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERupert1992127_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERupert1992127-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> although in several cases this resulted in employees bringing legal action against their employers, claiming that such seminars had infringed on their religious beliefs or damaged their psychological health.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERupert1992133_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERupert1992133-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> However, the use of spiritual techniques as a method for attaining <a href="/wiki/Profit_(economics)" title="Profit (economics)">profit</a> has been an issue of major dispute within the wider New Age movement,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199223Heelas199686_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199223Heelas199686-303">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> with prominent New Agers such as Spangler and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Fox_(priest)" title="Matthew Fox (priest)">Matthew Fox</a> criticising what they see as trends within the community that are narcissistic and lack a social conscience.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996358–361_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996358–361-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> In particular, the movement's commercial elements have caused problems given that they often conflict with its general economically-egalitarian ethos; as York highlighted, "a tension exists in New Age between socialistic egalitarianism and capitalistic private enterprise".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork199540_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork199540-305">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Given that it encourages individuals to choose <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_practices" class="mw-redirect" title="Spiritual practices">spiritual practices</a> on the grounds of personal preference and thus encourages them to behave as a consumer, the New Age has been considered to be well suited to modern society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge200434_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge200434-306">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_new-age_music_artists" title="List of new-age music artists">List of new-age music artists</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_ambient_artists" class="mw-redirect" title="List of ambient artists">List of ambient artists</a></div>
<p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/New_Age_music" class="mw-redirect" title="New Age music">New Age music</a></i> is applied, sometimes in a derogative manner, to forms of <a href="/wiki/Ambient_music" title="Ambient music">ambient music</a>, a genre that developed in the 1960s and was popularised in the 1970s, particularly with the work of <a href="/wiki/Brian_Eno" title="Brian Eno">Brian Eno</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge2004175_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge2004175-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> The genre's relaxing nature resulted in it becoming popular within New Age circles,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge2004175_307-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge2004175-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> with some forms of the genre having a specifically New Age orientation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge2004178_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge2004178-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup>
Studies have determined that new-age music can be an effective component of <a href="/wiki/Stress_management" title="Stress management">stress management</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The style began in the late 1960s and early 1970s with the works of free-form jazz groups recording on the <a href="/wiki/ECM_(record_label)" class="mw-redirect" title="ECM (record label)">ECM</a> label; such as <a href="/wiki/Oregon_(band)" title="Oregon (band)">Oregon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Paul_Winter_Consort" title="Paul Winter Consort">Paul Winter Consort</a>, and other pre-ambient bands; as well as ambient music performer Brian Eno, classical avant-garde musician <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Kobialka" title="Daniel Kobialka">Daniel Kobialka</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Birosik_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birosik-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Werkhoven_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Werkhoven-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Psychoacoustics" title="Psychoacoustics">psychoacoustic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Environments_(album_series)" title="Environments (album series)">environments</a></i> recordings of <a href="/wiki/Irv_Teibel" title="Irv Teibel">Irv Teibel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> In the early 1970s, it was mostly instrumental with both acoustic and electronic styles. New-age music evolved to include a wide range of styles from electronic <a href="/wiki/Space_music" title="Space music">space music</a> using <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizers</a> and acoustic instrumentals using <a href="/wiki/Native_American_flute" title="Native American flute">Native American flutes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drum" title="Drum">drums</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singing_bowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Singing bowl">singing bowls</a>, Australian <a href="/wiki/Didgeridoo" title="Didgeridoo">didgeridoos</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_music" title="World music">world music</a> sounds to spiritual <a href="/wiki/Chant" title="Chant">chanting</a> from other cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-Birosik_310-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birosik-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Werkhoven_311-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Werkhoven-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While many commentators have focused on the spiritual and cultural aspects of the New Age movement, it also has a political component. The New Age political movement became visible in the 1970s, peaked in the 1980s, and continued into the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> The sociologist of religion Steven Bruce noted that the New Age provides ideas on how to deal with "our socio-psychological problems".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199823_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199823-314">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup>  Scholar of religion James R. Lewis observed that, despite the common caricature of New Agers as narcissistic, "significant numbers" of them were "trying to make the planet a better place on which to live," <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis199211_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis199211-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> and scholar J. Gordon Melton's <i>New Age Encyclopedia</i> (1990) included an entry called "New Age politics". <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton1990323–325_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton1990323–325-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>  Some New Agers have entered the political system in an attempt to advocate for the societal transformation that the New Age promotes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideas">Ideas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Ideas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although New Age activists have been motivated by New Age concepts like holism, interconnectedness, monism, and environmentalism, their political ideas are diverse,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832_317-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> ranging from <a href="/wiki/Far_right_politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Far right politics">far-right</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservative</a> through to <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001367_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001367-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Accordingly, Kyle stated that "New Age politics is difficult to describe and categorize. The standard political labels—left or right, liberal or conservative–miss the mark."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832_317-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> MacKian suggested that the New Age operated as a form of "world-realigning infrapolitics" that undermines the <a href="/wiki/Disenchantment" title="Disenchantment">disenchantment</a> of modern Western society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012154_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKian2012154-318">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
