<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:288px;max-width:288px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:143px;max-width:143px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Mark_Satin_in_2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mark_Satin_in_2011.jpg/141px-Mark_Satin_in_2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="141" height="163" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mark_Satin_in_2011.jpg/212px-Mark_Satin_in_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mark_Satin_in_2011.jpg/282px-Mark_Satin_in_2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="414" data-file-height="478" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:141px;max-width:141px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:BENJAMIN_CREME_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/BENJAMIN_CREME_2006.jpg/139px-BENJAMIN_CREME_2006.jpg" decoding="async" width="139" height="164" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/BENJAMIN_CREME_2006.jpg/209px-BENJAMIN_CREME_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/BENJAMIN_CREME_2006.jpg/278px-BENJAMIN_CREME_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1181" data-file-height="1394" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="background-color:transparent">Writers who have espoused political ideas influenced by New Age perspectives included <a href="/wiki/Mark_Satin" title="Mark Satin">Mark Satin</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Creme" title="Benjamin Creme">Benjamin Creme</a> (right).</div></div></div></div>
<p>The extent to which New Age spokespeople mix religion and politics varies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b837_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b837-319">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> New Agers are often critical of the established political order, regarding it as "fragmented, unjust, hierarchical, patriarchal, and obsolete".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832_317-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> The New Ager <a href="/wiki/Mark_Satin" title="Mark Satin">Mark Satin</a> for instance spoke of "New Age politics" as a politically radical "third force" that was "neither left nor right". He believed that in contrast to the conventional political focus on the "institutional and economic symptoms" of society's problems, his "New Age politics" would focus on "psychocultural roots" of these issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b833_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b833-320">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> Ferguson regarded New Age politics as "a kind of Radical Centre", one that was "not neutral, not middle-of-the-road, but a view of the whole road."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832–833_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b832–833-321">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> Fritjof Capra argued that Western societies have become sclerotic because of their adherence to an outdated and mechanistic view of reality, which he calls the <i>Newtonian/Cartesian paradigm</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996107_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996107-322">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup>  In Capra's view, the West needs to develop an organic and ecological "systems view" of reality in order to successfully address its social and political issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996107_322-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996107-322">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Corinne_McLaughlin" title="Corinne McLaughlin">Corinne McLaughlin</a> argued that politics need not connote endless power struggles, that a new "spiritual politics" could attempt to synthesize opposing views on issues into higher levels of understanding.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many New Agers advocate <a href="/wiki/Globalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Globalisation">globalisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glocalization" title="Glocalization">localisation</a>, but reject <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> and the role of the <a href="/wiki/Nation-state" class="mw-redirect" title="Nation-state">nation-state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b842_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b842-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> Some New Age spokespeople have called for greater decentralisation and global unity, but are vague about how this might be achieved; others call for a global, centralised government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b842–843_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b842–843-325">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> Satin for example argued for a move away from the nation-state and towards self-governing regions that, through improved global communication networks, would help engender world unity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b843_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b843-326">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Creme" title="Benjamin Creme">Benjamin Creme</a> conversely argued that "the Christ," a great <a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">Avatar</a>, Maitreya, the <a href="/wiki/World_Teacher" class="mw-redirect" title="World Teacher">World Teacher</a>, expected by all the major religions as their "Awaited One," would return to the world and establish a strong, centralised global government in the form of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>; this would be politically re-organised along a spiritual hierarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b845_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b845-327">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> Kyle observed that New Agers often speak favourably of <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a> and citizens' involvement in policy making but are critical of <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Majority_rule" title="Majority rule">majority rule</a>, thus displaying elitist ideas to their thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b846_279-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle1995b846-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Groups">Groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_World_Alliance_1980.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Twenty people sitting in a circle" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/New_World_Alliance_1980.jpg/220px-New_World_Alliance_1980.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/New_World_Alliance_1980.jpg/330px-New_World_Alliance_1980.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/New_World_Alliance_1980.jpg/440px-New_World_Alliance_1980.jpg 2x" data-file-width="868" data-file-height="620" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_World_Alliance_1980.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/New_World_Alliance" title="New World Alliance">New World Alliance</a> was one of several New Age political groups in the 1970s and 1980s.</div></div></div>
<p>Scholars have noted several New Age political groups.  Self-Determination: A Personal/Political Network, lauded by Ferguson<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> and Satin,<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> was described at length by sociology of religion scholar Steven Tipton.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup>  Founded in 1975 by California state legislator <a href="/wiki/John_Vasconcellos" title="John Vasconcellos">John Vasconcellos</a> and others, it encouraged Californians to engage in personal growth work and political activities at the same time, especially at the grassroots level.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup>  Hanegraaff noted another California-based group, the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Noetic_Sciences" title="Institute of Noetic Sciences">Institute of Noetic Sciences</a>, headed by author <a href="/wiki/Willis_Harman" title="Willis Harman">Willis Harman</a>.  It advocated a change in consciousness – in "basic underlying assumptions" –  in order to come to grips with global crises.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996108–109_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996108–109-332">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup>  Kyle said that the New York City-based Planetary Citizens organization, headed by United Nations consultant and <i>Earth at Omega</i> author Donald Keys, sought to implement New Age political ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKyle199567_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKyle199567-333">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholar J. Gordon Melton and colleagues focused on the New World Alliance, a Washington, DC-based organization founded in 1979 by Mark Satin and others.  According to Melton et al., the Alliance tried to combine left- and right-wing ideas as well as personal growth work and political activities.  Group decision-making was facilitated by short periods of silence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton1990324_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton1990324-334">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup>  Sponsors of the Alliance's national political newsletter included Willis Harman and John Vasconcellos.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup>  Scholar James R. Lewis counted "Green politics" as one of the New Age's more visible activities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis199211_315-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis199211-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup>  One academic book claims that the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Green_Party_of_the_United_States" title="History of the Green Party of the United States">U.S. Green Party movement</a> began as an initiative of a handful of activists including <a href="/wiki/Charlene_Spretnak" title="Charlene Spretnak">Charlene Spretnak</a>, co-author of a "'new age' interpretation" of the German Green movement (Capra and Spretnak's <i>Green Politics</i>), and Mark Satin, author of <i>New Age Politics</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>  Another academic publication says Spretnak and Satin largely co-drafted the U.S. Greens' founding document, the "Ten Key Values" statement.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_21st_century">In the 21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: In the 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marianne_Williamson_(46644134).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Head shot of woman speaking from a podium" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Marianne_Williamson_%2846644134%29.jpg/180px-Marianne_Williamson_%2846644134%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Marianne_Williamson_%2846644134%29.jpg/270px-Marianne_Williamson_%2846644134%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Marianne_Williamson_%2846644134%29.jpg/360px-Marianne_Williamson_%2846644134%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1944" data-file-height="1949" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marianne_Williamson_(46644134).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Age author <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Williamson" title="Marianne Williamson">Marianne Williamson</a> campaigned for a seat in the U.S. Congress and for the Democratic Party nomination for president.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>While the term <i>New Age</i> may have fallen out of favor,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200174_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200174-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayAnderson2000188–189_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayAnderson2000188–189-339">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> scholar <a href="/wiki/George_Chryssides" title="George Chryssides">George Chryssides</a> notes that the New Age by whatever name is "still alive and active" in the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides200722_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides200722-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  In the realm of politics, New Ager Mark Satin's book <i>Radical Middle</i> (2004) reached out to mainstream liberals.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup>  York (2005) identified "key New Age spokespeople" including <a href="/wiki/William_Bloom" title="William Bloom">William Bloom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Satish_Kumar" title="Satish Kumar">Satish Kumar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Starhawk" title="Starhawk">Starhawk</a> who were emphasizing a link between spirituality and environmental consciousness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork200528_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork200528-342">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> Former <a href="/wiki/Esalen_Institute" title="Esalen Institute">Esalen Institute</a> staffer Stephen Dinan's <i>Sacred America, Sacred World</i>  (2016) prompted a long interview of Dinan in <i><a href="/wiki/Psychology_Today" title="Psychology Today">Psychology Today</a></i>, which called the book a "manifesto for our country's evolution that is both political and deeply spiritual".<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013 longtime New Age author Marianne Williamson launched a campaign for a seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a>, telling <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> that her type of spirituality was what American politics needed.<sup id="cite_ref-Lovett_344-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lovett-344">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup>  "America has swerved from its ethical center", she said.<sup id="cite_ref-Lovett_344-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lovett-344">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup>  Running as an independent in west Los Angeles, she finished fourth in her district's <a href="/wiki/Open_primaries_in_the_United_States" title="Open primaries in the United States">open primary election</a> with 13% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup>  In early 2019, Williamson announced her candidacy for the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> nomination for <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">president of the United States</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2020_United_States_presidential_election" title="2020 United States presidential election">2020 United States presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peele_347-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peele-347">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> A 5,300-word article about <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Williamson_2020_presidential_campaign" title="Marianne Williamson 2020 presidential campaign">her presidential campaign</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> said she had "plans to fix America with love. Tough love".<sup id="cite_ref-Peele_347-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peele-347">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_media">Popular media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Popular media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mainstream periodicals tended to be less than sympathetic; sociologist Paul Ray and psychologist Sherry Anderson discussed in their 2000 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cultural_Creatives" title="The Cultural Creatives">The Cultural Creatives</a></i>, what they called the media's "zest for attacking" New Age ideas, and offered the example of a 1996 <a href="/wiki/Lance_Morrow" title="Lance Morrow">Lance Morrow</a> essay in <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayAnderson2000188–189_339-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayAnderson2000188–189-339">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> Nearly a decade earlier, <i>Time</i> had run a long cover story critical of New Age culture; the cover featured a head shot of a famous actress beside the headline, "<b>Om....</b> THE NEW AGE starring Shirley MacLaine, faith healers, channelers, space travelers, and crystals galore".<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>  The story itself, by former <i><a href="/wiki/The_Saturday_Evening_Post" title="The Saturday Evening Post">Saturday Evening Post</a></i> editor <a href="/wiki/Otto_Friedrich" title="Otto Friedrich">Otto Friedrich</a>, was sub-titled, "A Strange Mix of Spirituality and Superstition Is Sweeping Across the Country".<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup>  In 1988, the magazine <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> ran a four-page critique of New Age culture and politics by journalist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bradley_(writer)" title="Richard Bradley (writer)">Richard Blow</a> entitled simply, "Moronic Convergence".<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some New Agers and New Age sympathizers responded to such criticisms.  For example, sympathizers Ray and Anderson said that much of it was an attempt to "stereotype" the movement for idealistic and spiritual change, and to cut back on its popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayAnderson2000188–189_339-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayAnderson2000188–189-339">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> New Age theoretician David Spangler tried to distance himself from what he called the "New Age glamour" of crystals, talk-show channelers, and other easily commercialized phenomena, and sought to underscore his commitment to the New Age as a vision of genuine social transformation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996104–105_351-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996104–105-351">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academia">Academia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Academia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wouter_Hanegraaff_2006_Alchemy_Conference.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Wouter_Hanegraaff_2006_Alchemy_Conference.jpg/220px-Wouter_Hanegraaff_2006_Alchemy_Conference.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Wouter_Hanegraaff_2006_Alchemy_Conference.jpg/330px-Wouter_Hanegraaff_2006_Alchemy_Conference.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Wouter_Hanegraaff_2006_Alchemy_Conference.jpg/440px-Wouter_Hanegraaff_2006_Alchemy_Conference.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1116" data-file-height="1156" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wouter_Hanegraaff_2006_Alchemy_Conference.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the first academics to study the New Age was Wouter Hanegraaff.</div></div></div>
<p>Initially, academic interest in the New Age was minimal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200120_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200120-352">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> The earliest academic studies of the New Age phenomenon were performed by specialists in the study of new religious movements such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ellwood" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Ellwood">Robert Ellwood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis19926Hanegraaff19963_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis19926Hanegraaff19963-353">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup> This research was often scanty because many scholars regarded the New Age as an insignificant cultural fad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisMelton1992x_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisMelton1992x-354">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> Having been influenced by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Anti-cult_movement" title="Anti-cult movement">anti-cult movement</a>, much of it was also largely negative and critical of New Age groups.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton199215_355-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton199215-355">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> The "first truly scholarly study" of the phenomenon was an <a href="/wiki/Edited_volume" title="Edited volume">edited volume</a> put together by James R. Lewis and J. Gordon Melton in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200120_352-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200120-352">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> From that point on, the number of published academic studies steadily increased.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200120_352-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200120-352">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1994, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Christoph_Bochinger&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Christoph Bochinger (page does not exist)">Christoph Bochinger</a> published his study of the New Age in Germany, <i>"New Age" und moderne Religion</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200120_352-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200120-352">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> This was followed by Michael York's sociological study in 1995 and Richard Kyle's U.S.-focused work in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200121_356-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200121-356">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> In 1996, Paul Heelas published a sociological study of the movement in Britain, being the first to discuss its relationship with business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas19965Hammer200121–22_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas19965Hammer200121–22-357">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup> That same year, Wouter Hanegraaff published <i>New Age Religion and Western Culture</i>, a historical analysis of New Age texts;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff19964–6_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff19964–6-358">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> Hammer later described it as having "a well-deserved reputation as the standard reference work on the New Age".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammer200122_359-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammer200122-359">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup> Most of these early studies were based on a textual analysis of New Age publications, rather than on an <a href="/wiki/Ethnography" title="Ethnography">ethnographic</a> analysis of its practitioners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a13_360-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffe2003a13-360">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sutcliffe and Gilhus argued that 'New Age studies' could be seen as having experienced two waves; in the first, scholars focused on "macro-level analyses of the content and boundaries" of the "movement", while the second wave featured "more variegated and contextualized studies of particular beliefs and practices".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffeGilhus20136_361-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffeGilhus20136-361">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> Sutcliffe and Gilhus have also expressed concern that, as of 2013, 'New Age studies' has yet to formulate a set of research questions scholars can pursue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffeGilhus20136_361-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffeGilhus20136-361">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup>
The New Age has proved a challenge for scholars of religion operating under more formative models of what "religion" is.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutcliffeGilhus20135_362-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutcliffeGilhus20135-362">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> By 2006, Heelas noted that the New Age was so vast and diverse that no scholar of the subject could hope to keep up with all of it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas200615_363-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas200615-363">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_perspectives">Christian perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Christian perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mainstream Christianity has typically rejected the ideas of the New Age;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides200721_364-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides200721-364">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup> Christian critiques often emphasise that the New Age places the human individual before God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200561–62_365-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelasWoodhead200561–62-365">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> Most published criticism of the New Age has been produced by Christians, particularly those on the religion's <a href="/wiki/Fundamentalist_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamentalist Christianity">fundamentalist wing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996201_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996201-366">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> In the United States, the New Age became a major concern of <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">evangelical</a> Christian groups in the 1980s, an attitude that influenced British evangelical groups.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHexham1992152_367-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHexham1992152-367">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> During that decade, evangelical writers such as <a href="/wiki/Constance_Cumbey" title="Constance Cumbey">Constance Cumbey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dave_Hunt_(Christian_apologist)" title="Dave Hunt (Christian apologist)">Dave Hunt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gary_North_(economist)" title="Gary North (economist)">Gary North</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Groothuis" title="Douglas Groothuis">Douglas Groothuis</a> published books criticising the New Age; a number propagated <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theories" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspiracy theories">conspiracy theories</a> regarding its origin and purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHexham1992154_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHexham1992154-368">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> The most successful such publication was <a href="/wiki/Frank_E._Peretti" title="Frank E. Peretti">Frank E. Peretti</a>'s 1986 novel <i><a href="/wiki/This_Present_Darkness" title="This Present Darkness">This Present Darkness</a></i>, which sold over a million copies; it depicted the New Age as being in league with <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a> and secular education as part of a conspiracy to overthrow Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHexham1992156_369-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHexham1992156-369">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Official responses to the New Age have been produced by major Christian organisations like the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist Church">Methodist Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides200721_364-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides200721-364">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup>
The Roman Catholic Church published <i><a href="/wiki/A_Christian_reflection_on_the_New_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="A Christian reflection on the New Age">A Christian reflection on the New Age</a></i> in 2003, following a six-year study; the 90-page document criticizes New Age practices such as yoga, meditation, <a href="/wiki/Feng_shui" title="Feng shui">feng shui</a>, and crystal healing.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Vatican_370-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Vatican-370">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup> According to the Vatican, euphoric states attained through New Age practices should not be confused with prayer or viewed as signs of God's presence.<sup id="cite_ref-NY_Times_Vatican_372-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY_Times_Vatican-372">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup> Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Paul_Poupard" title="Paul Poupard">Paul Poupard</a>, then-president of the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_Council_for_Culture" title="Pontifical Council for Culture">Pontifical Council for Culture</a>, said the "New Age is a misleading answer to the oldest hopes of man".<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Vatican_370-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Vatican-370">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup> Monsignor <a href="/wiki/Michael_L._Fitzgerald" title="Michael L. Fitzgerald">Michael Fitzgerald</a>, then-president of the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_Council_for_Interreligious_Dialogue" title="Pontifical Council for Interreligious Dialogue">Pontifical Council for Interreligious Dialogue</a>, stated at the Vatican conference on the document: the "Church avoids any concept that is close to those of the New Age".<sup id="cite_ref-Holy_See_373-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holy_See-373">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup> There are also other Christian groups that have adopted a more positive view of the New Age, among them the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Christaquarians&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Christaquarians (page does not exist)">Christaquarians</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Christians_Awakening_to_a_New_Awareness&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Christians Awakening to a New Awareness (page does not exist)">Christians Awakening to a New Awareness</a>, all of which believe that New Age ideas can enhance a person's Christian faith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChryssides200721–22_374-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChryssides200721–22-374">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_Pagan_perspectives">Contemporary Pagan perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Contemporary Pagan perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"Neopagan practices highlight the centrality of the relationship between humans and nature and reinvent religions of the past, while New Agers are more interested in transforming individual consciousness and shaping the future."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Religious studies scholar Sarah Pike<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike200418_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike200418-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>An issue of academic debate has been regarding the connection between the New Age and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Paganism" title="Modern Paganism">Modern Paganism</a>, sometimes termed "Neo-paganism".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp200410Partridge200479_375-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp200410Partridge200479-375">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup> The two phenomena have often been confused and conflated, particularly in Christian critiques.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp20048Partridge200479_376-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp20048Partridge200479-376">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup> Religious studies scholar Sarah Pike asserted that there was a "significant overlap" between the two religious movements,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPike2004vii_377-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPike2004vii-377">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Aidan_A._Kelly" title="Aidan A. Kelly">Aidan A. Kelly</a> stated that Paganism "parallels the New Age movement in some ways, differs sharply from it in others, and overlaps it in some minor ways".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly1992136_378-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly1992136-378">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup> Other scholars have identified them as distinct phenomena that share overlap and commonalities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001364–365Doyle_White20169_379-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001364–365Doyle_White20169-379">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup> Hanegraaff suggested that whereas various forms of contemporary Paganism were not part of the New Age movement – particularly those that pre-dated the movement – other Pagan religions and practices could be identified as New Age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199678_380-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199678-380">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup> Partridge portrayed both Paganism and the New Age as different streams of <a href="/wiki/Occulture" class="mw-redirect" title="Occulture">occulture</a> (<a href="/wiki/Occult" title="Occult">occult</a> culture) that merge at points.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge200478_381-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge200478-381">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Various differences between the two movements have been highlighted; the New Age movement focuses on an improved future, whereas the focus of Paganism is on the pre-Christian past.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly1992138_382-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly1992138-382">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the New Age movement typically propounds a <a href="/wiki/Universalism" title="Universalism">universalist</a> message that sees all religions as fundamentally the same, whereas Paganism stresses the difference between <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic religions</a> and those embracing a <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheistic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a> theology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly1992138_382-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly1992138-382">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup> While the New Age emphasises a light-centred image, Paganism acknowledges both light and dark, life and death, and recognises the savage side of the natural world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge200479_383-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge200479-383">&#91;383&#93;</a></sup> Many Pagans have sought to distance themselves from the New Age movement, even using "New Age" as an insult within their community, while conversely many involved in the New Age have expressed criticism of Paganism for emphasizing the material world over the spiritual.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle_White20169_384-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle_White20169-384">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup>
Many Pagans have expressed criticism of the high fees charged by New Age teachers, something not typically present in the Pagan movement,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly1992139Partridge200479_385-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly1992139Partridge200479-385">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup> with some Pagans pronouncing the word "newage" to rhyme with "<a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlainWallis20076_386-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlainWallis20076-386">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Western_and_indigenous_responses">Non-Western and indigenous responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Non-Western and indigenous responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"In the case of New Age, its solipsism, coupled with its advocacy of free market principles, opens the world's spiritual arena as an opportunity for spiritual exploitation and even capitalistic imperialism. Not only does it encourage a paradoxical homogenizing to the cultural standards of North Atlantic civilization, exemplified in its affirmation that 'we are all one', but it also carries an implicit judgement of inferior status for non-hegemonic cultures, inasmuch as they are not considered to be the ones who decide what is to be shared and what is not."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Sociologist Michael York<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001367–368_387-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001367–368-387">&#91;387&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>One of the most contentious aspects of the New Age has been its adoption of spiritual ideas and practises from other, particularly non-Western cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001367_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001367-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Its belief that all traditions are free for anyone to use, rather than the <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a> of particular communities, has resulted in New Agers adopting and marketing the practices of <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a> societies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001368_388-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001368-388">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup> These have included "Hawaiian <a href="/wiki/Kahuna" title="Kahuna">Kahuna</a> magic, Australian Aboriginal dream-working, South American Amerindian <i><a href="/wiki/Ayahuasca" title="Ayahuasca">ayahuasca</a></i> and <i>San Pedro</i> ceremony, Hindu Ayurveda and yoga, and Chinese Feng Shui, <a href="/wiki/Qigong" title="Qigong">Qi Gong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tai_chi" title="Tai chi">Tai Chi</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYork2001368_388-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork2001368-388">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The New Age has been accused of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_imperialism" title="Cultural imperialism">cultural imperialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_appropriation" title="Cultural appropriation">misappropriating</a> the sacred ceremonies, and abuse of the intellectual and cultural property of indigenous peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-LDNwar1_389-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LDNwar1-389">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hobson_390-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hobson-390">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aldred_291-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aldred-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996202_391-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas1996202-391">&#91;391&#93;</a></sup> Indigenous American spiritual leaders, such as Elders councils of the <a href="/wiki/Lakota_people" title="Lakota people">Lakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne" title="Cheyenne">Cheyenne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Navajo_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Navajo people">Navajo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Creek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hopi" title="Hopi">Hopi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe" title="Ojibwe">Chippewa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Haudenosaunee</a> have denounced New Age misappropriation of their sacred ceremonies<sup id="cite_ref-Rez5_392-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rez5-392">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup> and other <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_intellectual_property" title="Indigenous intellectual property">intellectual property</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-UNDRIP_393-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNDRIP-393">&#91;393&#93;</a></sup> stating that "[t]he value of these instructions and ceremonies [when led by unauthorized people] are questionable, maybe meaningless, and hurtful to the individual carrying false messages".<sup id="cite_ref-Rez5_392-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rez5-392">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup> Traditional leaders of the Lakota, <a href="/wiki/Dakota_people" title="Dakota people">Dakota</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Assiniboine" title="Assiniboine">Nakota</a> peoples have reached consensus<sup id="cite_ref-LDNwar1_389-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LDNwar1-389">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taliman1_394-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taliman1-394">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup> to reject "the expropriation of [their] ceremonial ways by non-Indians". They see the New Age movement as either not fully understanding, deliberately trivializing, or distorting their way of life,<sup id="cite_ref-Fenelon1998_395-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fenelon1998-395">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup> and have declared war on all such "<a href="/wiki/Plastic_shaman" title="Plastic shaman">plastic medicine people</a>" who are <a href="/wiki/Cultural_appropriation" title="Cultural appropriation">appropriating</a> their spiritual ways.<sup id="cite_ref-LDNwar1_389-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LDNwar1-389">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taliman1_394-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taliman1-394">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Indigenous leaders have spoken out against individuals from within their own communities who may go out into the world to become a "white man's shaman," and any "who are prostituting our spiritual ways for their own selfish gain, with no regard for the spiritual well-being of the people as a whole".<sup id="cite_ref-Fenelon1998_395-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fenelon1998-395">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup> The term "<a href="/wiki/Plastic_shaman" title="Plastic shaman">plastic shaman</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Plastic_medicine_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Plastic medicine people">plastic medicine people</a>" has been applied to outsiders who identify themselves as shamans, holy people, or other traditional spiritual leaders, but who have no genuine connection to the traditions or cultures they claim to represent.<sup id="cite_ref-Hobson_390-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hobson-390">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aldred_291-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aldred-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uwash_396-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uwash-396">&#91;396&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_writers_and_activists">Political writers and activists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Political writers and activists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"New Age politics might be seen not as a wayward, pathological creature of the New Left's imagination, but as a political innocent in candid, questioning dialogue with the unclaimed mainstream territory of progressive, rather than atomistic, individualism.  Indeed, if we were to examine some of the social and political threads that run through the aery fabric of New Age thinking, we would find certain themes that resonate with the necessary conditions for a left version of progressive individualism.  Generally speaking, New Age addresses its adherents as active participants, with a measure of control over their everyday lives. ...  The New Age 'person' is also in many respects an individual whose personal growth is indissociable from the environment; a link fleshed out in a variety of ecotopian stories and romances.  So, too, the small-scale imperative of New Age's cooperative communitarianism brings with it a host of potentially critical positions. ..."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Scholar of cultural studies <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ross_(sociologist)" title="Andrew Ross (sociologist)">Andrew Ross</a>, 1991<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoss199169_397-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoss199169-397">&#91;397&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Toward the end of the 20th century, some social and political analysts and activists were arguing that the New Age political perspective had something to offer mainstream society.<sup id="cite_ref-398" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-398">&#91;398&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-399" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-399">&#91;399&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-400" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-400">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> In 1987, some political scientists launched the "Section on Ecological and Transformational Politics" of the <a href="/wiki/American_Political_Science_Association" title="American Political Science Association">American Political Science Association</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-401" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-401">&#91;401&#93;</a></sup> and an academic book prepared by three of them stated that the "transformational politics" concept was meant to subsume such terms as <i>new age</i> and <i>new paradigm</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-402" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-402">&#91;402&#93;</a></sup>  In 1991, scholar of cultural studies <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ross_(sociologist)" title="Andrew Ross (sociologist)">Andrew Ross</a> suggested that New Age political ideas – however muddled and naïve – could help <a href="/wiki/Progressivism_in_the_United_States#Progressivism_in_the_21st_century" title="Progressivism in the United States">progressives</a> construct an appealing alternative to both atomistic individualism and self-denying collectivism. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoss199165–72_(&quot;New_Age_politics&quot;_sub-chapter)_403-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoss199165–72_(&quot;New_Age_politics&quot;_sub-chapter)-403">&#91;403&#93;</a></sup>  In 2005, British researcher Stuart Rose urged scholars of alternative religions to pay more attention to the New Age's interest in such topics as "new socio-political thinking" and "New Economics",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2005b165_404-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2005b165-404">&#91;404&#93;</a></sup> topics Rose discussed in his book <i>Transforming the World: Bringing the New Age Into Focus</i>, issued by a European academic publisher.<sup id="cite_ref-405" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-405">&#91;405&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other political thinkers and activists saw New Age politics less positively.  On the political right, author <a href="/wiki/George_Weigel" title="George Weigel">George Weigel</a> argued that New Age politics was just a retooled and pastel-colored version of leftism.<sup id="cite_ref-406" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-406">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup>  Conservative evangelical writer Douglas Groothuis, discussed by scholars Hexham<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHexham199254_407-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHexham199254-407">&#91;407&#93;</a></sup> and Kemp,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp200452_408-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp200452-408">&#91;408&#93;</a></sup> warned that New Age politics could lead to an oppressive world government.<sup id="cite_ref-409" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-409">&#91;409&#93;</a></sup>  On the left, scholars argued that New Age politics was an oxymoron: that personal growth has little or nothing to do with political change.<sup id="cite_ref-410" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-410">&#91;410&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-411" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-411">&#91;411&#93;</a></sup>  One political scientist said New Age politics fails to recognize the reality of economic and political power.<sup id="cite_ref-412" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-412">&#91;412&#93;</a></sup>  Another academic, <a href="/wiki/Dana_L._Cloud" title="Dana L. Cloud">Dana L. Cloud</a>, wrote a lengthy critique of New Age politics as a political ideology;<sup id="cite_ref-413" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-413">&#91;413&#93;</a></sup> she faulted it for not being opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Capitalist_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Capitalist system">capitalist system</a>, or to liberal individualism.<sup id="cite_ref-414" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-414">&#91;414&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A criticism of New Age often made by leftists is that its focus on individualism deflects participants from engaging in socio-political activism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas20068_415-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas20068-415">&#91;415&#93;</a></sup> This perspective regards New Age as a manifestation of consumerism that promotes <a href="/wiki/Elitism" title="Elitism">elitism</a> and indulgence by allowing wealthier people to affirm their socio-economic status through consuming New Age products and therapies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeelas20066–7_416-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeelas20066–7-416">&#91;416&#93;</a></sup>  New Agers who do engage in socio-political activism have also been criticized.  Journalist <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Wasserman" title="Harvey Wasserman">Harvey Wasserman</a> suggested that New Age activists were too averse to social conflict to be effective politically.<sup id="cite_ref-417" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-417">&#91;417&#93;</a></sup> Melton et al. found that New Age activists' commitment to the often frustrating process of consensus decision-making led to "extended meetings and minimal results",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton1990324_334-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton1990324-334">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup> and a pair of futurists concluded that one once-promising New Age activist group had been both "too visionary and too vague" to last.<sup id="cite_ref-418" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-418">&#91;418&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
